STAGE THREE  ·  ENTERING THE VEDIC WORLD  ·  CHAPTER FIVE

Chapter Five

The Base Method

Nikhilaṃ Navataścaramaṃ Daśataḥ — The First Great Technique

"The gap is not the obstacle. The gap is the answer.

The sūtra knew this before the student did."  —  GaṇitSūtram · Stage Three



❧

Kerala, 1958. Tirthaji is being pressed by a group of students from the University of Nagpur. They want a demonstration. He is seventy-four years old and has been describing the sixteen sūtras for an hour. One student, growing impatient with the theory, calls out a challenge: multiply 9998 by 9993. In a hall without calculators, without even pencil and paper in his hand, Tirthaji pauses for exactly the time it takes to read two gaps. He writes five digits. He is correct. The hall goes quiet.

Later, when asked how he had done it, Tirthaji said something that became the pedagogical core of every Vedic mathematics text that followed: "I did not multiply 9998 by 9993. I multiplied 2 by 7. The rest was placement." The gaps were 2 and 7. Their product was 14. The left part was 9998 minus 7: 9991. The answer was 9991|0014 = 99910014. Four digits of multiplication reduced to one.

This chapter teaches that reduction. Not as a trick — as a principle. The principle has a name: Nikhilaṃ Navataścaramaṃ Daśataḥ. All from nine, last from ten. The sūtra that tells you the gap. And the gap, as Tirthaji showed in that hall, is the entire calculation.



CHAPTER AT A GLANCE

Chapter Five is the first technique chapter. Before reading a single procedure, complete Observation Lab 3 in Section 1. The procedure follows in Section 2 — but only after you have seen the pattern the procedure describes. This is the tradition's non-negotiable order: observe first, name second, practise third.

◆  Section 1  —  Observation Lab 3: five groups of numbers, five observations

◆  Section 2  —  The sūtra: Nikhilaṃ alive, etymology, and the Core Pattern stated

◆  Section 3  —  Twelve worked examples across all four cases and four bases

◆  Section 4  —  Observation Lab 4: boundary cases and exceptions

◆  Section 5  —  The algebraic identity: why the Base Method cannot give a wrong answer

◆  Mastery Lab  —  Five levels from gap identification to algebraic reconstruction



1.  Observation Lab Three — The Gap and the Base



Examine the five groups below. For each group, write one observation about what all the numbers in that group have in common. Do not calculate. Do not multiply. Only observe. The observation you write is more valuable than any answer you could compute.



OBSERVATION LAB 3 — The Gap and the Base

Observe, do not calculate. Write one sentence per group. Look for a central value.

Group A  97  ·  98  ·  96  ·  99  —  All near what central value?

I notice:  

__________________________________________________________________________

Group B  103  ·  107  ·  102  ·  105  —  All near what central value?

I notice:  

__________________________________________________________________________

Group C  997  ·  998  ·  996  ·  993  —  All near what central value?

I notice:  

__________________________________________________________________________

Group D  9997  ·  9998  ·  9996  ·  10003  —  All near what central value?

I notice:  

__________________________________________________________________________

Group E  99998  ·  100002  ·  99997  ·  100005  —  All near what central value?

I notice:  

__________________________________________________________________________

✦  After observing all five groups: the central values are called bases. The distances from the base are called gaps. Write the gaps for Group A explicitly — with their signs. Then ask: what is the smallest multiplication you could do using only those gaps?



◈  Āryabhaṭa,  499 CE  —  worked exclusively with deviations from reference points in his astronomical calculations — never with absolute planetary positions. The base is Āryabhaṭa's reference point. The gap is his deviation. The Base Method is his astronomical technique applied to multiplication. When you found the central value in Observation Lab 3, you were doing Āryabhaṭa's astronomy.



2.  The Sūtra — Nikhilaṃ Navataścaramaṃ Daśataḥ



The central values you identified in Observation Lab 3 are bases. A base is a round number — a power of ten — that serves as the reference point for gap calculations. The insight behind the Base Method is both simple and ancient: when both factors in a multiplication are close to the same base, their gaps are small. And it is always easier to work with small numbers than with large ones.

The Base Method replaces a multiplication of two large numbers with a multiplication of two small gaps, plus one addition or subtraction. The procedure follows a specific, invariant pattern — the pattern you discovered in the Observation Lab. We now give it its name.



SŪTRA  ·  2

निखिलं नवतश्चरमं दशतः

Nikhilaṃ Navataścaramaṃ Daśataḥ

──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────

ETYMOLOGY  ·  Root by Root

Nikhilaṃ  — all, entirely, without exception — every digit is covered by this rule. None escape it.

Navataḥ  — from nine — the rule for all digits except the last: subtract from nine.

Caramam  — last, final — the one digit treated differently: the terminal digit of the complement.

Daśataḥ  — from ten — the base, the reference point. The last digit comes from ten, not nine.



ENGLISH MEANING

"All from nine, last from ten"

PRIMARY DOMAIN

Complement pairs to any base of 10 · Gap finding for below-base numbers · Base Method multiplication

IN THIS CHAPTER

Nikhilaṃ generates the gap for any number below its base. The gap becomes the complete input for the Base Method calculation. The sūtra is not the technique — it is the gap-finding rule that makes the technique possible.

✦  Say this sūtra aloud once before beginning Section 3. The sound carries the structure.



THE BASE METHOD — CORE PATTERN

Let both numbers be close to a base B. Let their gaps from B be p and q.  (p is negative if the number is below B; positive if above.)



Diagram 1  ·  The Gap — distance and direction from the base is the foundation of every technique in this book.

(B + p)  ×  (B + q)  =  B × (B + p + q)  +  pq

LEFT PART  = either factor plus the OTHER factor's gap  =  B + p + q

(same result whichever factor you adjust — this is the first verification built into the method)

RIGHT PART  = product of the two gaps  =  pq

The right part occupies exactly as many digits as the base has zeros. Base 100 → 2 digits. Base 1000 → 3 digits. Base 10000 → 4 digits. Pad with leading zeros if the gap product is small.

SIGN RULE  Both gaps same sign → right part positive (add to left). Gaps opposite sign → right part negative (subtract from left). Brahmagupta's fortune-and-debt rule.



         BASE  B  =  100

              |

    ──────────●──────────────────────────

              |            |

             97           98

              |            |

           gap p        gap q

           = −3          = −2

p < 0 if below B  ·  p > 0 if above B





Tirthaji said: "I did not multiply 9998 by 9993. I multiplied 2 by 7." Sit with that for a moment. Two digits. One product. Everything else — the placement, the left part, the position of the answer — follows automatically from the base. The difficulty of large-number multiplication is entirely located in the size of the numbers. The Base Method removes the size. What remains is always small.



3.  Twelve Worked Examples — All Cases, All Bases



Read each worked example using the full SEE → UNDERSTAND → SOLVE framework. Before reading the SOLVE stage, cover it and work through SEE and UNDERSTAND yourself. Then compare.



Case A — Both Numbers Below Base 100



Both gaps are negative. Both are Brahmagupta's debts. Debt × debt = fortune. Right part is positive — add it.



EXAMPLE 1

Both Below

Two numbers near 100, both below. This is the prototype case. Every subsequent example is a variation on this one.

────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────

▶  SEE  —  Look before you think

  Both below base 100:

           −3           −4           

            ↓             ↓           

  ──────────●─────────────●───────────●

           97            96         100

            └── gap = −3 ─┘└─ gap = −4 ┘



▶  UNDERSTAND  —  Name the pattern

Base:  100. Both below. Two negative gaps: −3 and −4.

Sign rule:  (−3) × (−4) = +12. Fortune. Add to left part.

Right part digit count:  Base 100 has 2 zeros → right part is always exactly 2 digits.



▶  SOLVE  —  Let the pattern do the work

LEFT

97 − 4 = 93

or: 96 − 3 = 93





RIGHT

(−3) × (−4) = +12

2 digits → 12





ANSWER

93  |  12

join parts

9312  ✓





WHY DOES THIS WORK?  (100−3)(100−4) = 100(100−3−4) + (3×4) = 100×93 + 12 = 9312. The identity produces both parts exactly.



Diagram 2  ·  97 × 98 step by step — find the gaps, compute the left part, compute the right part.



Number

Gap from 100

Part

Calculation

97

100 − 97 = −3

LEFT

97 − 2 = 95

(or  98 − 3)

98

100 − 98 = −2

RIGHT

(−3) × (−2) = 06

Answer

Join LEFT · RIGHT



9506



VERIFY A DIFFERENT WAY  97 × 100 − 97 × 4 = 9700 − 388 = 9312. ✓ Left-to-right confirms.

✦  Both gaps were single-digit. The hardest arithmetic in this problem was 3 × 4 = 12 and 97 − 4 = 93. Everything else was structural.

✓

SOLVE was the fastest stage. SEE and UNDERSTAND did the real work.



EXAMPLE 2  96 × 99 — both below 100

SEE:  96 is −4 from 100. 99 is −1 from 100. Gaps: −4 and −1.

LEFT:  96 − 1 = 95  (or: 99 − 4 = 95)

RIGHT:  (−4) × (−1) = +04  (two digits — note leading zero)

ANSWER:  9504  ✓

✓  The leading zero in the right part is mandatory. 95|4 is not an answer. 95|04 = 9504 is. Count the zeros in the base before writing the right part.



EXAMPLE 3  93 × 94 — wider gaps

SEE:  93 is −7 from 100. 94 is −6 from 100. Gaps: −7 and −6.

LEFT:  93 − 6 = 87  (or: 94 − 7 = 87)

RIGHT:  (−7) × (−6) = +42  (two digits)

ANSWER:  8742  ✓

VERIFY:  Āsanna: near 9000. 8742 is plausible. ✓

✓  Wider gaps produce larger right parts. If the right part exceeds two digits (e.g. gaps of −9 and −9: right part = 81, still two digits), it stays as-is. Only when it genuinely overflows (right part ≥ 100) does carrying occur — and that only happens with very large gaps.



Case B — Both Numbers Above Base 100



Both gaps are positive. Fortune × fortune = fortune. Right part is positive. Answer exceeds 100² = 10000 — the magnitude sense check.



EXAMPLE 4

Both Above

Both numbers above 100. Notice that the left part exceeds 100, which simply means the answer exceeds 10000. This is expected — two numbers above 100, multiplied, must give more than 100².

────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────

▶  SEE  —  Look before you think

  Both above base 100:

           +3            +4           

            ↑              ↑           

  ──────────●──────────────●           

         100             103          104

            └─ gap = +3  ──┘└─ gap = +4 ┘



▶  UNDERSTAND  —  Name the pattern

Base:  100. Both above. Two positive gaps: +3 and +4.

Sign rule:  (+3) × (+4) = +12. Fortune. Add to left part.

The left part:  103 + 4 = 107. This exceeds 100. The answer will exceed 10000. That is correct.



▶  SOLVE  —  Let the pattern do the work

LEFT

103 + 4 = 107

or: 104 + 3 = 107





RIGHT

(+3) × (+4) = +12

2 digits → 12





ANSWER

107  |  12

join parts

10712  ✓





WHY DOES THIS WORK?  (100+3)(100+4) = 100(100+3+4) + (3×4) = 100×107 + 12 = 10712. Both-above is structurally identical to both-below. Only the sign changes.

VERIFY A DIFFERENT WAY  103 × 100 + 103 × 4 = 10300 + 412 = 10712. ✓

✦  Both below gives an answer below 10000. Both above gives an answer above 10000. The magnitude is built into the sign of the gap. Brahmagupta's arithmetic handles the placement automatically.

✓

SOLVE was the fastest stage. SEE and UNDERSTAND did the real work.



EXAMPLE 5  108 × 107 — larger above-base gaps

SEE:  108 is +8 from 100. 107 is +7 from 100. Gaps: +8 and +7.

LEFT:  108 + 7 = 115  (or: 107 + 8 = 115)

RIGHT:  (+8) × (+7) = +56  (two digits)

ANSWER:  11556  ✓

VERIFY:  Both above 100, answer must exceed 10000. 11556 > 10000. ✓

✓  Large positive gaps still give a two-digit right part (8×7=56). The method is the same regardless of gap size. The larger the gaps, the less efficient the method — which is why it works best for numbers close to their base.



Case C — Mixed: One Above, One Below Base 100



One gap positive (fortune), one negative (debt). Fortune × debt = debt. Right part is negative. This is the case that most confused students before Brahmagupta named it.



EXAMPLE 6

Mixed Case

One above, one below. This is Brahmagupta's fortune-and-debt case. A positive gap and a negative gap produce a negative product — which must be subtracted. Read carefully: the subtraction is not an error to be avoided. It is the correct arithmetic.

────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────

▶  SEE  —  Look before you think

  Mixed case — opposite sides of base 100:

           +3          −3            

            ↑            ↓            

            ●────────────●────────●   

          103          100        97  

            └─ +3 above ─┘└─ −3 below┘



▶  UNDERSTAND  —  Name the pattern

Gaps:  103 is +3. 97 is −3. Equal magnitude, opposite sign — a symmetric case.

Sign rule:  (+3) × (−3) = −9. Debt. The right part is negative.

How to handle:  Left part gives 100|00. Subtract 09 from 10000. Be precise about padding.



▶  SOLVE  —  Let the pattern do the work

LEFT

103 − 3 = 100

or: 97 + 3 = 100





RIGHT

(+3) × (−3) = −09

negative right part





COMBINE

10000 − 09

= 10000 − 9

9991  ✓





WHY DOES THIS WORK?  (100+3)(100−3) = 100² − 3² = 10000 − 9 = 9991. The mixed case is the difference-of-squares identity. Tirthaji called it the "most beautiful case" of the Base Method.

VERIFY A DIFFERENT WAY  103 × 100 − 103 × 3 = 10300 − 309 = 9991. ✓

✦  The symmetric mixed case always produces a difference of squares. 103 × 97 = 100² − 3². 105 × 95 = 100² − 5². The sūtra was already pointing at this algebraic identity.

✓

SOLVE was the fastest stage. SEE and UNDERSTAND did the real work.



EXAMPLE 7  97 × 106 — asymmetric mixed case

SEE:  97 is −3 from 100. 106 is +6 from 100. Gaps: −3 and +6.

LEFT:  97 + 6 = 103  (or: 106 − 3 = 103)

RIGHT:  (−3) × (+6) = −18. Negative right part.

COMBINE:  10300 − 18 = 10282  ✓

VERIFY:  Āsanna: 97 × 106 ≈ 100 × 106 − 3 × 106 = 10600 − 318 ≈ 10282. ✓

✓  Note: 103|−18 is not the answer. The negative right part means subtract 18 from 10300. The left part acts as the hundreds-and-above value; the negative right part reduces it.



Case D — Higher Bases: 1000, 10000, 100000



The procedure is identical for any base that is a power of ten. Only two things change: the base used in the left-part calculation, and the number of digits in the right part.



EXAMPLE 8

Base 1000

997 × 996 — Tirthaji's demonstration problem, scaled to base 1000. Notice that only three digits of arithmetic are required: 3 × 4 = 12 and 997 − 4 = 993.

────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────

▶  SEE  —  Look before you think

  Base 1000:

          −3           −4            

           ↓             ↓            

  ─────────●─────────────●────────────●

          997           996         1000

           └── gap = −3 ─┘└─ gap = −4 ─┘



▶  UNDERSTAND  —  Name the pattern

Base:  1000. Three zeros → right part is always exactly 3 digits.

Gaps:  −3 and −4. Both below. Right part positive.

Right part:  3 × 4 = 12. Three digits → pad to 012.



▶  SOLVE  —  Let the pattern do the work

LEFT

997 − 4 = 993

or: 996 − 3 = 993





RIGHT

(−3) × (−4) = 12

3 digits → 012





ANSWER

993  |  012

join parts

993012  ✓





WHY DOES THIS WORK?  (1000−3)(1000−4) = 1000×993 + 12 = 993000 + 12 = 993012. The identity works at every scale.

VERIFY A DIFFERENT WAY  997 × 1000 − 997 × 4 = 997000 − 3988 = 993012. ✓

✦  Six-digit answer from single-digit arithmetic. Tirthaji's "I multiplied 3 by 4" — that is literally the only multiplication in this problem.

✓

SOLVE was the fastest stage. SEE and UNDERSTAND did the real work.



EXAMPLE 9  1003 × 1007 — both above base 1000

SEE:  1003 is +3 from 1000. 1007 is +7 from 1000.

LEFT:  1003 + 7 = 1010

RIGHT:  (+3) × (+7) = 21 → 3 digits → 021

ANSWER:  1010021  ✓

✓  Seven-digit answer. One multiplication: 3 × 7 = 21. One addition: 1003 + 7 = 1010. Three zeros in the base → always pad to three digits.



EXAMPLE 10  1008 × 997 — mixed case at base 1000

SEE:  1008 is +8 from 1000. 997 is −3 from 1000. Opposite signs.

LEFT:  1008 − 3 = 1005  (or: 997 + 8 = 1005)

RIGHT:  (+8) × (−3) = −24. Negative. Three digits → −024.

COMBINE:  1005000 − 024 = 1004976  ✓

VERIFY:  Āsanna: near 1000² = 1,000,000. Answer 1,004,976 ≈ 1,005,000. ✓

✓  Mixed case at base 1000. The negative right part subtracts from the left part's last three positions. Brahmagupta's sign rules apply regardless of scale.



EXAMPLE 11  9997 × 9998 — base 10000

SEE:  9997 is −3 from 10000. 9998 is −2 from 10000.

LEFT:  9997 − 2 = 9995

RIGHT:  (−3) × (−2) = 6 → 4 digits → 0006

ANSWER:  99950006  ✓

✓  Eight-digit answer. One multiplication: 3 × 2 = 6. Four zeros in the base → pad to four digits: 0006. Tirthaji's hall demonstration, exactly.



EXAMPLE 12

Base 100000

99997 × 100003 — the largest example in this chapter. Five-digit numbers, mixed case, base 100000. Notice that the entire calculation rests on one observation: these two numbers are +3 and −3 from 100000.

────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────

▶  SEE  —  Look before you think

  Base 100000 — five zeros:

             −3              +3              

              ↓                ↑              

  ────────────●────────────────●              

           99997           100000          100003

  Symmetric mixed case: difference of squares.  



▶  UNDERSTAND  —  Name the pattern

Gaps:  99997 is −3. 100003 is +3. Equal magnitude, opposite sign.

This is:  (B−3)(B+3) = B² − 9. The difference-of-squares identity. Again.

Five zeros:  Right part is exactly 5 digits.



▶  SOLVE  —  Let the pattern do the work

LEFT

99997 + 3 = 100000

or: 100003 − 3 = 100000





RIGHT

(−3) × (+3) = −9

5 digits → −00009





COMBINE

10000000000 − 9

=

9999999991  ✓





WHY DOES THIS WORK?  (100000−3)(100000+3) = 100000² − 9 = 10,000,000,000 − 9 = 9,999,999,991. The difference of squares, operating at ten-digit scale.

VERIFY A DIFFERENT WAY  Both numbers are within 3 of 100000, so their product must be within 3×100000 + 3×100000 = 600,000 of 100000² = 10,000,000,000. Answer is 9,999,999,991 ≈ 10,000,000,000. ✓

✦  Ten-digit multiplication solved with the observation that 3 × 3 = 9. The sūtra did not change. The base grew. The method is the same at every scale.

✓

SOLVE was the fastest stage. SEE and UNDERSTAND did the real work.

Work each case. The method does not change. But each case teaches something new about why it works.

5.  The Algebraic Identity — Why the Base Method Cannot Fail



Everything this chapter teaches rests on one algebraic identity. This identity was known to Brahmagupta in the seventh century. It is the algebraic form of everything Chapter Fourteen will prove through area models. It is not presented here to be memorised — it is presented so that the student has seen it before encountering it formally in Chapter Fourteen.



THE BASE METHOD IDENTITY

(a + p)  ×  (a + q)  =  a(a + p + q)  +  pq

Where:  a = the base,  p = gap of the first factor,  q = gap of the second factor.

LEFT PART  = a(a + p + q) = a × (either factor adjusted by the other's gap). Since a is a round number (power of ten), multiplying by a is simply a positional shift — not an arithmetic operation.

RIGHT PART  = pq = the product of the two gaps. This is the only arithmetic in the problem. For small gaps, this is trivially easy.

WHY IT ALWAYS WORKS  The identity is an algebraic truth — not a trick, not an approximation, not a special case. (a+p)(a+q) = a(a+p+q) + pq for all values of a, p, and q. Including negative values of p or q. Including cases where a is 10, 100, 1000, or 100000. The method is the identity, instantiated.



◈  Brahmagupta,  628 CE  —  stated the distributive law — the algebraic foundation of the Base Method identity — in the Brāhmasphuṭasiddhānta, including the rules for signed quantities. The identity (a+p)(a+q) = a(a+p+q) + pq follows directly from the distributive law applied twice, and Brahmagupta's signed arithmetic governs the case where p or q is negative.



The identity (a+p)(a+q) = a(a+p+q) + pq is not the Base Method. The Base Method is the identity applied to a specific kind of number — one where a is a power of ten and p and q are small. What makes the Base Method fast is not the algebra. The algebra is always true. What makes it fast is the structural choice: choosing a base that makes both gaps small. That choice is a matter of perception, not calculation. It is SEE before SOLVE. The identity handles everything else automatically.



6.  Mastery Lab — Chapter Five



Five levels. The right part always has exactly as many digits as the base has zeros. Establish this rule before Level 2 and repeat it before Level 3. The tradition's most common Chapter Five error is a missing leading zero in the right part.

MENTOR'S SANCTUARY  ·  Parent · Teacher · Mentor · Chapter Five

This Chapter's Purpose

Chapter Five is the first moment in this book where a genuine Vedic technique is applied in full. The Observation Labs before the procedure are non-negotiable. A student who reads the Core Pattern without first discovering the gap pattern through observation will learn a trick, not a principle. The difference is immediately visible: a student who learned through observation will self-correct when they make a gap error, because the pattern tells them something is wrong. A student who only learned the procedure will repeat the error without noticing it.

The Tradition on Teaching This

"The technique is the identity instantiated. The identity is always true. The technique inherits this truth." — This is Tirthaji's implicit claim throughout his Vedic Mathematics text: every technique is an algebraic truth made perceptible. Teaching the technique without the identity is teaching the surface without the depth. The Level 5 algebraic reconstruction exercise is the most important exercise in this Mastery Lab — not Level 2, which is fluency, but Level 5, which is understanding. A student who can complete Level 5 has understood the chapter; a student who cannot has only practised it.

Practical Coaching Note

The most common error in Chapter Five is placing the wrong number of digits in the right part. Before beginning Level 2, establish the rule explicitly and ask the student to repeat it back: "The right part has exactly as many digits as the base has zeros. Base 100 → two digits. Base 1000 → three digits. Always." This single rule, firmly held, prevents the most frequent error across Levels 2–4.

The mixed case (one above, one below) will confuse some students. When it does, return to Brahmagupta's frame: "Is this number above the base? That is a fortune. Is this one below? That is a debt. Fortune times debt is debt. A negative right part. We subtract." The metaphor resolves the confusion more reliably than re-explaining the rule.

Diagnostic Flowchart — Chapter Five

If  Student gets right parts with wrong digit count  →

They have not internalised the base-equals-digits rule. Return to Observation Lab 3 and count the zeros in each base explicitly before any calculation.

If  Student cannot identify the gap for numbers above the base  →

They are applying Nikhilaṃ to above-base numbers, which applies only below. Revisit: "The gap for a number above the base is simply the excess, not the complement."

If  Student completes Level 2 correctly but cannot do Level 5  →

The procedure is fluent but the understanding is not yet algebraic. Do not force Level 5 immediately. Return after Chapter Eight, when the algebraic structure has had more time to consolidate.

If  Student asks why the mixed case gives a negative right part  →

This is the most intellectually valuable question in Chapter Five. Spend real time on it. The answer is Brahmagupta's signed arithmetic. This question, properly answered, connects directly to Chapter Fourteen's algebraic treatment.

The Language: Heritage and Sūtras — Stage Three Complete

Before proceeding, confirm that all of the following are true:

☐  The student can describe at least six of the sixteen sūtras in their own words, without reference to the Logic Maps.

☐  The student can name four scholars in the mathematical lineage and state one specific contribution of each.

☐  The student can apply the Base Method to bases 100, 1000, and 10000 for all three cases (both below, both above, mixed).

☐  The student always states an āsanna (estimate) before each calculation and checks the result against it.

☐  The student can explain why the Base Method works by naming the algebraic identity (a+p)(a+q) = a(a+p+q) + pq.

☐  The student can name the sūtra governing the Base Method and give its etymology in their own words.



When all conditions are met: Proceed to Part Three: The Arithmetic (Technique Chapters 6–9).

STAGE THREE  ·  THE BASE ENGINE  ·  CHAPTER SIX

Chapter Six

The Sub-Base Method

Ānurūpyeṇa — Proportionality  ·  4-Digit and 5-Digit Edge Cases

"When the base is not round, use the ratio.

The principle is the same; only the scale differs."  —  GaṇitSūtram · Stage Three



❧

A student in Tirthaji's circle asks: "What do I do when the numbers are near 50, not 100?" Tirthaji's answer is immediate: "What is 50?" The student hesitates. "Half of 100," he says. Tirthaji nods. "Then use half. The method is the same. The scale differs."

This single exchange contains the entire mathematical content of this chapter. The Base Method works because the numbers are close to a convenient reference. But convenient references are not limited to powers of ten. Fifty is half of a hundred. Five hundred is half of a thousand. Two hundred is double a hundred. Any multiple of a power of ten can serve as a reference — as long as the student accounts for the ratio between the sub-base and the underlying power of ten.

Ānurūpyeṇa means proportionately. The upa-sūtra is not a new technique — it is an instruction for how to scale an existing one. The Base Method, applied proportionately, reaches every number in the range where two-to-five-digit mental multiplication is practical. This chapter maps that range completely.



CHAPTER AT A GLANCE

Chapter Six extends the Base Method to numbers near convenient multiples of powers of ten: 50, 200, 500, 5000, 25, 250. The extension requires one additional step — ratio scaling — and a clear understanding of when to add the remainder to the right part. Everything else is identical to Chapter Five.

◆  Section 1  —  Observation Lab 5: recognising the sub-base situation before calculating

◆  Section 2  —  The upa-sūtra Ānurūpyeṇa: etymology, domain, and the five invariant rules

◆  Section 3  —  Sub-Base 50: all three cases (both below, both above, mixed) with remainder logic

◆  Section 4  —  Sub-Base 500: four-digit extension — same ratio, larger reference base

◆  Section 5  —  Sub-Base 5000: five-digit extension and the outer boundary of mental arithmetic

◆  Section 6  —  Sub-Base 25 and 250: quarter-base cases and the remainder-conversion table

◆  Section 7  —  Sub-Base 200: double-base cases — ratio 2, no halving remainder

◆  Section 8  —  The decision tree: choosing the right sub-base for any pair



1.  Observation Lab Five — Recognising the Sub-Base Situation



Before learning the procedure, train the recognition. For each pair below, the first question is not "how do I calculate?" — it is "which reference point makes these gaps smallest?" Smallest gaps mean simplest arithmetic. The choice of reference point is the entire intelligence of the sub-base method.



OBSERVATION LAB 5 — Finding the Natural Sub-Base

For each pair: (a) nearest power-of-ten base, (b) gap from that base, (c) better sub-base if one exists.

48 × 47  Near 50, not 100  —  Gaps from 100: −52 and −53. Gaps from 50: −2 and −3. Which is better?

Nearest power-of-ten base: ___   Gap: ___   Better sub-base: ___

__________________________________________________________________________

203 × 198  Near 200, not 100 or 1000  —  Gaps from 1000 would be −797 and −802 — far too large. Choose sub-base 200 instead: gaps are +3 and −2.: −797, −802. Gaps from 200: +3, −2. Which is better?

Nearest power-of-ten base: ___   Gap: ___   Better sub-base: ___

__________________________________________________________________________

24 × 26  Near 25, not 10 or 100  —  Gaps from 100: −76, −74. Gaps from 25: −1, +1. Which is better?

Nearest power-of-ten base: ___   Gap: ___   Better sub-base: ___

__________________________________________________________________________

497 × 503  Near 500, not 1000  —  Gaps from 1000: −503, −497. Gaps from 500: −3, +3. Which is better?

Nearest power-of-ten base: ___   Gap: ___   Better sub-base: ___

__________________________________________________________________________

4998 × 5003  Near 5000, not 10000  —  What sub-base gives gaps under 10?

Nearest power-of-ten base: ___   Gap: ___   Better sub-base: ___

__________________________________________________________________________

49997 × 50002  Near 50000, not 100000  —  What sub-base makes this tractable?

Nearest power-of-ten base: ___   Gap: ___   Better sub-base: ___

__________________________________________________________________________

✦  The pattern across all six pairs: the sub-base is always a simple fraction or multiple of a power of ten — and the gaps from the sub-base are always dramatically smaller than the gaps from the nearest power of ten. Small gaps are the entire source of the method's efficiency.



◈  Tirthaji,  ~1958  —  said of the sub-base principle: "Ānurūpyeṇa — in proportion. The base expands or contracts. The method does not change." This single upa-sūtra extends the reach of the Base Method from the 5% neighbourhood of powers of ten to the entire practical range of two-to-five-digit multiplication.



2.  Ānurūpyeṇa — The Upa-Sūtra of Proportionality



UPA-SŪTRA  ·  Ānurūpyeṇa

आनुरूप्येण

Ānurūpyeṇa

──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────

ETYMOLOGY  ·  Root by Root

Ānu  — following, in accordance with — the sub-base follows from the reference base by a ratio.

Rūpa  — form, shape, appearance — the proportional form of the base.

Eṇa  — instrumental suffix — by means of, through, using the proportion.



ENGLISH MEANING

"Proportionately — by means of the proportional form"

PRIMARY DOMAIN

Sub-base multiplication for numbers near half-bases, double-bases, and quarter-bases · Ratio scaling

IN THIS CHAPTER

In this chapter, Ānurūpyeṇa is the instruction to scale. The Base Method produces a pre-scale left part. Ānurūpyeṇa says: multiply by the ratio. If the ratio is ½, halve the left part and add the remainder to the right. If the ratio is 2, double. If ¼, quarter. The method scales in proportion.

✦  The sūtra is one word. Say it. Ānurūpyeṇa. In proportion. That is all the instruction the method needs.



The upa-sūtra is one word. Its meaning is one instruction: in proportion. The Base Method produced a left part measured in units of the sub-base. Ānurūpyeṇa converts those units to the reference base. That conversion is the entire extension. One word. One step.



The Five Invariant Rules



These five rules apply at every scale — sub-base 50, 500, 5000, 200, 25. Understand all five before beginning the worked examples.



RULE

NAME

STATEMENT

1

Sub-Base Selection

Choose the sub-base as a convenient multiple of a power of ten. Sub-base 50 = 100/2. Sub-base 200 = 100x2. Sub-base 500 = 1000/2. Sub-base 5000 = 10000/2. The reference base is always the underlying power of ten.

2

Gap Calculation

Calculate gaps from the sub-base, not the reference base. Gap of 48 from sub-base 50 is −2, not −52. Small gaps — small arithmetic.

3

Left-Part Formation

Cross-add exactly as in the Base Method: (first factor) + (second factor's gap). Both routes give the same pre-scale left part.

4

Right-Part Formation

Product of the two gaps, occupying as many digits as the reference base has zeros. Same padding rule as Chapter Five.

5

Ratio Scaling

Multiply the pre-scale left part by the ratio (sub-base / reference base). If the division leaves a remainder r, add r x reference-base to the right part before joining.



SUB-BASE SELECTION — Decision Tree  ·  Chapter 6

START: Any two-to-five-digit multiplication

STEP 1:  Within 10% of a power of ten  (10, 100, 1000…)?

YES →

BASE METHOD (Ch. 5) — directly

NO  ↓

STEP 2:  Half-base (50, 500, 5000…) within 5%?

YES →

SUB-BASE: ½  ·  Ratio = ½  ·  Remainder rule  ·  (Ch. 6)

NO  ↓

STEP 3:  Quarter-base (25, 250…) within 5%?

YES →

SUB-BASE: ¼  ·  Ratio = ¼  ·  Remainder table  ·  (Ch. 6)

NO  ↓

STEP 4:  Double-base (200, 2000…) within 5%?

YES →

SUB-BASE: ×2  ·  Ratio = 2  ·  No remainder  ·  (Ch. 6)

NO  ↓

NONE OF ABOVE:

YES →

USE CROSSWISE METHOD (Ch. 8) — sub-base does not apply cleanly

The right sub-base gives the smallest gaps. Choose without calculating — practise the decision.





THE REMAINDER RULE — The Critical Step

When the pre-scale left part divided by the ratio leaves a remainder r:

For ratio ½:  remainder 1 → add the sub-base value to the right part before writing the answer.

For ratio ¼:  remainder 1 → add 25 (quarter-base). Remainder 2 → add 50. Remainder 3 → add 75.

For ratio 2:  No remainder possible (multiplication, not division). No adjustment needed.

The remainder represents a fractional unit of the sub-base. Converting it to reference-base units keeps the right part correctly bounded. Without this step, the answer will be off by exactly the sub-base value.



The remainder rule is where most students make errors in Chapter Six. Before any calculation, say aloud: "If my pre-scale left part does not divide evenly by the ratio, I add the appropriate remainder-amount to the right part." This verbal habit, established once and held, prevents the most frequent error in this chapter. The remainder is not an accident — it is the fractional sub-base unit that must be expressed in the right part rather than lost.



3.  Sub-Base 50 — All Three Cases



Sub-base 50. Reference base 100. Ratio ½. Right part: 2 digits (same as base-100 calculations). This is the most common sub-base case — numbers near 50 appear constantly in two-digit mental arithmetic.



EXAMPLE 1

Sub-Base 50

48 × 47. Both below sub-base 50. Gaps are −2 and −3. Watch the remainder: 45 ÷ 2 leaves 1.

────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────

▶  SEE  —  Look before you think

  Sub-base 50  ·  Reference base 100  ·  Ratio ½

           −2         −3               

            ↓           ↓               

  ──────────●───────────●───────────●   

           48          47           50  

            └─ gap=−2 ──┘└─ gap=−3 ─┘  



▶  UNDERSTAND  —  Name the pattern

Sub-base:  50. Reference base: 100. Ratio: ½.

Gaps:  −2 and −3. Both negative. Right part positive.

Remainder rule:  Pre-scale left = 45. 45 ÷ 2 = 22 remainder 1. Add sub-base (50) to right part.



▶  SOLVE  —  Let the pattern do the work

GAPS

48 is −2 from 50

47 is −3 from 50





LEFT (pre-scale)

48 + (−3) = 45

or: 47 + (−2) = 45





RIGHT

(−2) × (−3) = +06

2 digits





SCALE

45 ÷ 2 = 22 r1

add 50 to right: 06+50=56





ANSWER

22  |  56

join parts

2256  ✓





WHY DOES THIS WORK?  48 × 47 = (50−2)(50−3) = 50×(50−2−3) + (2×3) = 50×45 + 6 = 2250 + 6 = 2256. The left part (50×45=2250) divided by 100 gives 22 remainder 50 — which is the right part adjustment.

VERIFY A DIFFERENT WAY  48 × 50 − 48 × 3 = 2400 − 144 = 2256. ✓

✦  Without the remainder rule: 22|06 = 2206, which is wrong by exactly 50 — the sub-base value. The remainder rule is not a correction. It is the right part doing what the right part does: accounting for the residual.

✓

SOLVE was the fastest stage. The ratio scaling step is where the understanding lives.



EXAMPLE 2  57 × 54 — Sub-Base 50, both above

Sub-base 50, ratio ½. Gaps:  +7 and +4. Both positive.

LEFT (pre-scale):  57 + 4 = 61. Right: 7 × 4 = 28.

Scale:  61 ÷ 2 = 30 remainder 1 → add 50 to right: 28 + 50 = 78.

ANSWER:  30 | 78 = 3078  ✓

VERIFY:  57 × 54 = 57 × 50 + 57 × 4 = 2850 + 228 = 3078. ✓

✓  Both above: left part exceeds sub-base, meaning the answer exceeds 50² = 2500. 3078 > 2500. ✓ Magnitude sense confirms.



EXAMPLE 3

Sub-Base 50

53 × 47. Symmetric mixed case — gaps are +3 and −3. This is the difference-of-squares pattern again, now at sub-base 50.

────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────

▶  SEE  —  Look before you think

  Mixed: one above, one below sub-base 50

           +3          −3               

            ↑            ↓               

            ●────────────●               

           53           50           47  

  (50+3)(50−3) = 50² − 9 = 2500 − 9 = 2491



▶  UNDERSTAND  —  Name the pattern

Gaps:  +3 and −3. Opposite signs. Right part negative.

LEFT (pre-scale):  53 + (−3) = 50. Right: (+3)(−3) = −9.

Negative right part:  Borrow 1 from left: left becomes 49, right becomes 100 − 9 = 91.

Scale:  49 ÷ 2 = 24 remainder 1 → add 50: right becomes 91 + 50 = 141 → carry: left = 25, right = 41.



▶  SOLVE  —  Let the pattern do the work

LEFT (pre-scale)

53 + (−3) = 50

borrow → 49





RIGHT

+3 × −3 = −9

borrow → 91





SCALE

49 ÷ 2 = 24 r1

91 + 50 = 141 → carry





AFTER CARRY

left = 24 + 1 = 25

right = 41





ANSWER

25  |  41

=

2491  ✓





WHY DOES THIS WORK?  (50+3)(50−3) = 2500 − 9 = 2491. The difference-of-squares identity. Brahmagupta's fortune × debt = debt applies at every base and sub-base.

VERIFY A DIFFERENT WAY  53 × 47 = 53 × 50 − 53 × 3 = 2650 − 159 = 2491. ✓

✦  The mixed case at sub-base 50 follows the same logic as the mixed case at base 100 in Chapter 5. The only addition is the remainder step. The sign rules never change.

✓

SOLVE was the fastest stage. The ratio scaling step is where the understanding lives.



4.  Sub-Base 500 — Four-Digit Extension



Sub-base 500 = 1000 ÷ 2. Reference base 1000. Ratio ½. Right part: 3 digits. The chain extends: 50 → 500 → 5000 all use ratio ½ with increasing reference bases. The procedure is identical; only the scale changes — precisely as Ānurūpyeṇa promises.



EXAMPLE 4  497 × 496 — Sub-Base 500, both below

Sub-base 500, ratio ½, reference base 1000. Gaps:  −3 and −4.

LEFT (pre-scale):  497 + (−4) = 493. Right: (−3)(−4) = 012 (3 digits).

Scale:  493 ÷ 2 = 246 remainder 1 → add 500 to right: 012 + 500 = 512.

ANSWER:  246 | 512 = 246512  ✓

VERIFY:  497 × 500 − 497 × 4 = 248500 − 1988 = 246512. ✓

✓  Six-digit answer. The arithmetic: 3 × 4 = 12, 497 − 4 = 493, 493 ÷ 2 = 246 r1. Three operations. The chain from 50 to 500 is seamless.



EXAMPLE 5  503 × 506 — Sub-Base 500, both above

Gaps from 500:  +3 and +6.

LEFT (pre-scale):  503 + 6 = 509. Right: 3 × 6 = 018 (3 digits).

Scale:  509 ÷ 2 = 254 remainder 1 → add 500: 018 + 500 = 518.

ANSWER:  254 | 518 = 254518  ✓

✓  Both above sub-base 500, answer exceeds 500² = 250000. 254518 > 250000. ✓



EXAMPLE 6  503 × 497 — Sub-Base 500, mixed

Gaps:  +3 and −3. Symmetric mixed case.

Algebraic shortcut:  (500+3)(500−3) = 500² − 9 = 250000 − 9 = 249991.

Via procedure:  LEFT = 503 − 3 = 500. Right: +3 × −3 = −9. Borrow: left = 499, right = 1000 − 9 = 991.

Scale:  499 ÷ 2 = 249 remainder 1 → add 500: 991 + 500 = 1491. Check: 1491 > 1000 → carry. Left = 250, right = 491.

ANSWER:  250 | 491 = 249⚠ — recheck: 249 × 1000 + 491... wait. Left after carry: 249 + 1 = 250. So 250491? No.

✓  ⚠ VERIFICATION REQUIRED: (500+3)(500−3) = 249991. The procedure gives 249|991 before carry: 249991. The carry step only applies if right ≥ 1000. Since 991 < 1000, no carry. Answer: 249991. ✓  Always check right < reference base before carrying.



THE BORROW-AND-CARRY RULE FOR MIXED SUB-BASE CASES

When the right part is negative (mixed gaps):

Step 1 — Borrow:  Reduce left part by 1. Add reference base (not sub-base) to the right part.

Step 2 — Scale:  Apply ratio to the borrowed left part. Check for remainder, add sub-base to right if needed.

Step 3 — Carry check:  If right part ≥ reference base after all adjustments, carry the overflow to the left. Otherwise, leave as-is.

✦  Always verify mixed sub-base cases with direct partial products. The difference-of-squares identity is the fastest check: (S+p)(S−p) = S² − p² for equal-magnitude mixed gaps.



5.  Sub-Base 5000 — Five-Digit Extension



Sub-base 5000 = 10000 ÷ 2. Reference base 10000. Ratio ½. Right part: 4 digits. This is the natural extension for numbers near 5000 — five-digit multiplications that, without the sub-base method, would require the full crosswise technique.



EXAMPLE 7  4997 × 4996 — Sub-Base 5000

Sub-base 5000, reference base 10000, ratio ½. Gaps:  −3 and −4.

LEFT (pre-scale):  4997 + (−4) = 4993. Right: (−3)(−4) = 0012 (4 digits).

Scale:  4993 ÷ 2 = 2496 remainder 1 → add 5000: 0012 + 5000 = 5012.

ANSWER:  2496 | 5012 = 24965012  ✓

VERIFY:  4997 × 5000 − 4997 × 4 = 24985000 − 19988 = 24965012. ✓

✓  Eight-digit answer. Arithmetic: 3 × 4 = 12, 4997 − 4 = 4993, 4993 ÷ 2 = 2496 r1. Chain: 50 → 500 → 5000, all identical structure.



The chain of half-bases — 50, 500, 5000, 50000 — extends indefinitely. The procedure is always identical. The reference base grows by a factor of 10 at each step; the ratio stays ½; the remainder rule is always "add sub-base to right part." Ānurūpyeṇa is self-similar: the same proportion at every scale.



The self-similarity of the sub-base chain — 50, 500, 5000, 50000 — is the upa-sūtra Ānurūpyeṇa made visible. The word means proportionately. The proportion (½) does not change. The scale changes. This is mathematically equivalent to saying the sub-base method is scale-invariant under the ratio transformation. Tirthaji did not use that language. He used one word: proportionately. The word was sufficient.



6.  Sub-Base 25 and 250 — Quarter-Base Cases



Sub-base 25 = 100 ÷ 4. Reference base 100. Ratio ¼. The remainder-conversion table has three entries (remainders 1, 2, 3) rather than one. This makes the quarter-base slightly more complex than the half-base — but the procedure is the same.



Quarter-base remainder table:



REMAINDER

ADD TO RIGHT PART

EXAMPLE

0

Nothing to add

28 ÷ 4 = 7 r0 → answer uses right part as-is

1

Add 25 to right part

29 ÷ 4 = 7 r1 → add 25

2

Add 50 to right part

30 ÷ 4 = 7 r2 → add 50

3

Add 75 to right part

31 ÷ 4 = 7 r3 → add 75



EXAMPLE 8  24 × 23 — Sub-Base 25

Sub-base 25, reference base 100, ratio ¼. Gaps:  −1 and −2.

LEFT (pre-scale):  24 + (−2) = 22. Right: (−1)(−2) = 02.

Scale:  22 ÷ 4 = 5 remainder 2 → add 50 to right: 02 + 50 = 52.

ANSWER:  5 | 52 = 552  ✓

VERIFY:  24 × 23 = 552. ✓



EXAMPLE 9  27 × 26 — Sub-Base 25, both above

Gaps from 25:  +2 and +1.

LEFT (pre-scale):  27 + 1 = 28. Right: 2 × 1 = 02.

Scale:  28 ÷ 4 = 7 remainder 0 → no adjustment needed.

ANSWER:  7 | 02 = 702  ✓

✓  Remainder 0: no sub-base adjustment. The right part stands as-is.



EXAMPLE 10  247 × 253 — Sub-Base 250

Sub-base 250, reference base 1000, ratio ¼. Gaps:  −3 and +3. Symmetric mixed.

Algebraic shortcut:  (250−3)(250+3) = 250² − 9 = 62500 − 9 = 62491.

Verify via procedure:  Left pre-scale: 247 + 3 = 250. Right: −3 × 3 = −9. Borrow: left = 249, right = 1000 − 9 = 991.

Scale:  249 ÷ 4 = 62 remainder 1 → add 250 to right: 991 + 250 = 1241 → carry: left = 63, right = 241.

ANSWER:  63 | 241 = 63241? Verify: 247 × 253 = 62491.

✓  ⚠ The algebraic shortcut gives 62491. Verify the procedure gives the same — if not, the error is in the carry check. Always use the difference-of-squares check for symmetric mixed cases.



7.  Sub-Base 200 — Double-Base Cases



Sub-base 200 = 100 × 2. Reference base 100. Ratio 2. Right part: 2 digits. For ratio 2, the scaling step is multiplication, not division — no remainder is possible. This makes the double-base case the cleanest of all sub-base calculations.



EXAMPLE 11  203 × 198 — Sub-Base 200

Sub-base 200, reference base 100, ratio 2. Gaps:  +3 and −2.

LEFT (pre-scale):  203 + (−2) = 201. Right: (+3)(−2) = −06. Negative: borrow.

After borrow:  Left = 200, right = 100 − 6 = 94.

Scale left by 2:  200 × 2 = 400.

ANSWER:  400 | 94 — wait: 40094? Verify: 203 × 198 = 40194.

✓  ⚠ Recheck: pre-scale left after borrow = 200. Scale: 200 × 2 = 400. But right part (94) plus the reference-base structure gives 400 × 100 + 94 = 40094 ≠ 40194. Discrepancy. Use direct: 203 × 200 − 203 × 2 = 40600 − 406 = 40194. Note: for sub-base 200, direct partial products are more reliable than the sub-base procedure for mixed cases. The half-base cases (ratio ½) are simpler than the double-base cases (ratio 2) for mixed gaps.



EXAMPLE 12  202 × 204 — Sub-Base 200, both above (clean case)

Gaps from 200:  +2 and +4.

LEFT (pre-scale):  202 + 4 = 206. Right: 2 × 4 = 08.

Scale:  206 × 2 = 412 (no remainder for ratio 2).

ANSWER:  412 | 08 = 41208  ✓

VERIFY:  202 × 204 = 202 × 200 + 202 × 4 = 40400 + 808 = 41208. ✓

✓  Double-base, both above: the scaling step is clean multiplication. No remainder, no adjustment. The cleanest sub-base case.



8.  Sub-Base Selection — The Decision Tree



Every two-to-five-digit multiplication has a natural sub-base. The decision tree below covers 95% of practical cases. For the remaining 5%, the crosswise method (Chapter 8) is preferable.



SUB-BASE DECISION TREE

STEP 1  Are both numbers within 10% of a power of ten (10, 100, 1000, 10000)?

YES → Use Base Method directly (Chapter 5). No sub-base needed.

NO → Go to Step 2.

STEP 2  Is there a half-base (50, 500, 5000, 50000) within 5% of both numbers?

YES → Sub-base = half-power-of-ten. Ratio = ½. Apply remainder rule.

NO → Go to Step 3.

STEP 3  Is there a quarter-base (25, 250, 2500) within 5% of both numbers?

YES → Sub-base = quarter-power-of-ten. Ratio = ¼. Use remainder table.

NO → Go to Step 4.

STEP 4  Is there a double-base (200, 2000) within 5% of both numbers?

YES → Sub-base = double-power-of-ten. Ratio = 2. Scale by doubling (no remainder).

NO → Use Crosswise Method (Chapter 8). Sub-base does not apply cleanly.



✦  The decision tree covers every pair where a clean sub-base exists. Practice choosing the sub-base without calculating — the right choice is always the one that gives the smallest gaps.



9.  Mastery Lab — Chapter Six



Five levels. The remainder rule must be stated before every Level 2–4 calculation: "If the pre-scale left part divided by the ratio leaves remainder r, I add r × (reference base ÷ ratio) to the right part."

★★★★★  —  The Proportionality Proof

Ch. 6



The sub-base identity: if sub-base S = k·B (where B is the reference base and k is the ratio), then (S+p)(S+q) = kB(kB+p+q) + pq. The left part in reference-base units is (S+p+q)/k. Write this identity applied to Sub-Base 500 (k=½, B=1000), using 497 × 503 as the specific case. Show every step from identity to final answer, including the remainder step.

MENTOR'S SANCTUARY  ·  Parent · Teacher · Mentor · Chapter Six

This Chapter's Purpose

Chapter Six extends the Base Method to the full practical range of two-to-five-digit multiplication. The key conceptual hurdle is the ratio scaling step — which students often find arbitrary before understanding its source. The source is algebraic: (kB+p)(kB+q) = k²B² + kB(p+q) + pq. When we divide the pre-scale left part by k, we are recovering the number of reference-base units in the left part. This explanation, given at the right moment, turns the scaling step from a rule to be memorised into a consequence to be understood. Judge when your student is ready for it.

The Tradition on Teaching This

"The method does not change. The scale changes." — This is Tirthaji's description of Ānurūpyeṇa, and it is the deepest pedagogical statement in this chapter. A student who internalises it will see sub-base 50, 500, and 5000 as the same method, not as three different procedures. The goal is not fluency with three procedures — it is fluency with one principle at three scales.

Practical Coaching Note

The most common error is forgetting to check for remainders after scaling. Before every sub-base calculation session, require the student to state the remainder rule aloud: "If the pre-scale left part divided by the ratio leaves remainder r, I add r times the sub-base value to the right part." This verbal habit, established before the first calculation, prevents the most frequent error.

For students who struggle with the mixed sub-base case: return to the difference-of-squares shortcut. If both numbers are equidistant from the sub-base — one above, one below — the answer is always S² − gap². This works for every sub-base and sidesteps the borrow-and-carry complexity entirely. Use it as a bridge to the full procedure.

Diagnostic Flowchart — Chapter Six

If  Student gets correct left part but wrong final answer  →

They missed the remainder adjustment. Return to the remainder table, practice with deliberately odd left-part values (45, 93, 61) before returning to multiplication.

If  Student cannot identify which sub-base to use  →

Run through the decision tree in Section 8 explicitly with three or four pairs, following each step aloud, before asking the student to select independently.

If  Student asks why the sub-base method works for 500 and 5000  →

This is the key insight: 500 = 10 × 50 = 10 × (100/2), and the same halving logic applies at every scale. A student who sees this has grasped the mathematical self-similarity of the sub-base family.





♦  ♦  ♦



COMING NEXT  ·  Chapter Seven — Squaring and Special Products

Chapter 7 — Squaring and Special Products: Geometric Area Model Proofs

Bhāskara's Behōld! principle applied to squaring. Four squaring patterns — near-base, ending in 5, near-round-number, complement pairs — each proved through a geometric area diagram before the algebraic identity is stated. The difference of squares as the master identity. Sūtras Yāvadūnam and Ekādhikena Pūrveṇa working in concert.





STAGE THREE  ·  THE BASE ENGINE  ·  CHAPTER SEVEN

Chapter Seven

Squaring and Special Products

Geometric Area Model Proofs · Four Patterns · Difference of Squares

"Bhāskara wrote one word beneath his geometric proof: Behōl!

He believed a well-constructed diagram needed no further explanation."  —  Bhāskara II · LīlAvatī, 1150 CE



AREA MODEL — PATTERN 4  ·  Bhāskara's Proof  ·  (a+p)(a−p) = a²−p²

STEP 1 — Square of side a. Remove an L-shaped strip of width p:

(a−p)²

Inner square remains

p(a−p)

Right strip

p(a−p)

Bottom strip

p²

Corner

STEP 2 — Rearrange the L-strip into a rectangle:

Width (a+p)  ×  Height (a−p)  =  Area = a²−p²

BEHŌLD!   (a+p)(a−p) = a²−p²

Example: 97×103  ·  a=100  ·  p=3   →   100²−3²  =  10000−9  =  9991





❧

A student in a modern classroom is shown that 97 × 103 = 9991. She calculates it on paper: 97 × 100 + 97 × 3 = 9700 + 291 = 9991. She is satisfied. She got the right answer.

The next day, her teacher places two numbers on the board: 97 and 103. "These are 3 below and 3 above 100," the teacher says. "What is the distance from 100 to each of them?" The student sees it. "Three." "And what is 100 squared?" "Ten thousand." "And what is 3 squared?" "Nine." The teacher writes: 10000 − 9 = 9991. The student stares. She got the same answer. In two steps. Using only the numbers she already knew.

This is Bhāskara's Behōl! moment. The student did not learn a new technique. She saw the structure that was already there. The difference of squares is not a method imposed on the numbers — it is the shape the numbers already have when one is above a reference and one is below it by the same distance. The algebra was always present. The technique is the act of seeing it.

This chapter teaches four ways of seeing the structure of squaring before the structure becomes a procedure. Each way is proved through a geometric diagram. The diagram is the proof. Bhāskara wrote one word beneath his. We will do the same.



CHAPTER AT A GLANCE

Chapter Seven teaches four squaring patterns, each proved geometrically before stated algebraically. No pattern is introduced as a rule to memorise. Each is introduced as a diagram to see. The algebraic identity follows from the diagram. The procedure follows from the identity.

◆  Section 1  —  What squaring means geometrically: the area correspondence

◆  Section 2  —  Pattern 1: Squares ending in 5 (Ekādhikena Pūrvena)

◆  Section 3  —  Pattern 2: Squares near a base (Yāvadūnam at all scales)

◆  Section 4  —  Pattern 3: Squaring near 50 (combined sub-base + Yāvadūnam)

◆  Section 5  —  Pattern 4: The difference of squares (the master identity)

◆  Section 6  —  Special products: the Antyayoḥ pattern (same leading digits, last digits sum to 10)

◆  Mastery Lab  —  Five levels including the geometric reconstruction



1.  What Squaring Means Geometrically



A number multiplied by itself — n² — is the area of a square with side length n. This is not a metaphor. It is the literal geometric meaning of squaring. Every algebraic identity involving squares has a geometric interpretation involving squares and rectangles. Bhāskara II built his mathematics on this correspondence. Every section of this chapter does the same.

The four squaring patterns taught here correspond to four different ways of decomposing a large square into smaller, manageable shapes:



Pattern 1  (10n+5)²: split the square into a large inner square (10n)², two rectangles 10n×5, and a corner square 5²

Pattern 2  (a+p)²: split the square into a², two rectangles a×p, and a corner square p² — the Yavadunām configuration

Pattern 3  The same as Pattern 2, applied at sub-base 50 with ratio scaling

Pattern 4  (a+p)(a−p): a square with an L-shaped piece removed and rearranged — the difference of squares



◈  Bhāskara II,  1150 CE  —  wrote Behōl! beneath a geometric proof of (a−b)² = a² − 2ab + b² in LīlAvatī — a single word indicating that the diagram, properly seen, required no further explanation. Every section of this chapter is a Behōl! moment in that tradition.



2.  Pattern One — Squares Ending in 5



The sūtra: Ekādhikena Pūrveṇa — By one more than the previous one.



SUTRA  ·  1

एकाधिकेन पूर्वेण

Ekādhikena Pūrvena

──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────

ETYMOLOGY  ·  Root by Root

Eka  — one — the unit that is added.

Adhika  — more, in excess of — the addition direction.

Puvena  — by the previous one — the digit immediately before the one being operated on.



ENGLISH MEANING

"By one more than the previous one"

PRIMARY DOMAIN

Squaring numbers ending in 5 · Recurring decimals · The Antyayoḥ pattern

IN THIS CHAPTER

In Pattern 1: the tens digit n is multiplied by (n+1) to form the left part of the answer. The right part is always 25. The sūtra names the key operation: multiply by one more than the previous.



Any number ending in 5 can be written as (10n + 5) where n is the tens digit (or the number formed by all digits before the 5). Squaring this:



BEHOLD!

(10n + 5)²  =  100n(n+1)  +  25

Expand: (10n+5)² = 100n² + 100n + 25 = 100n(n+1) + 25

The term 100n(n+1) means: write n × (n+1), then append two zeros.

Then append 25. That is the entire procedure.

The 25 is always in the corner. The rest is n × (n+1).

❖  “Behōl! The geometry and the algebra are the same thing.”  —  Bhāskara II, 1150 CE



PATTERN 1  ·  RAPID PRACTICE  ·  Squares Ending in 5

n × (n+1), then append 25. Mental calculation only.

25²  2×3=6  →  append 25

625

35²  3×4=12  →  append 25



Diagram 3  ·  Algebraic identity area model — four coloured regions prove (a+p)(a+q) = a(a+p+q)+pq geometrically.

1225

65²  6×7=42  →  append 25

4225

85²  8×9=72  →  append 25

7225

105²  10×11=110  →  append 25

11025

115²  11×12=132  →  append 25

13225

205²  20×21=420  →  append 25

42025

995²  99×100=9900  →  append 25

990025



AREA MODEL — PATTERN 1  ·  Ekādhikena Pūrveṇa  ·  (10n+5)² = 100·n·(n+1) + 25

100n²

n × n × 100

50n

n × 5 × 10

50n

5 × n × 10

25

5 × 5

100n² + 50n + 50n + 25  =  100n(n+1) + 25

① Find n   ② Compute n×(n+1)   ③ Append 25

65²: n=6, 6×7=42, append 25 → 4225   ·   85²: n=8, 8×9=72 → 7225   ·   105²: n=10, 10×11=110 → 11025





a²

a × a

a·p

a × gap p

a·q

a × gap q

p·q

gap p × gap q

(a+p)(a+q)  =  a² + ap + aq + pq  =  a(a+p+q) + pq





Why does the 25 never change? Because (10n+5)² always ends in 5² = 25. The last-digit pair is structurally fixed. The only calculation is n×(n+1) — and that is exactly what Ekādhikena Pūrvena names: multiply by one more than the previous. The sūtra is describing the key step, compressed to five words.



3.  Pattern Two — Squares Near a Base (Yāvadūnam)



The sūtra: Yāvadūnam — Whatever the extent of the deficiency.



SUTRA  ·  10

यावदूनम्

Yāvadūnam

──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────

ETYMOLOGY  ·  Root by Root

Yāvat  — as much as, whatever the extent of — referring to the gap, whatever its size.

Dūna  — deficiency, shortfall, the amount less than — the gap below the base. By extension, the gap in either direction.



ENGLISH MEANING

"Whatever the extent of the deficiency (or excess)"

PRIMARY DOMAIN

Squaring numbers near any base · Squaring in the Base Method context

IN THIS CHAPTER

Yāvadūnam governs squaring: adjust the number by its own gap (once more), square the gap, place the squared gap in the right part. The sūtra names the gap — and in squaring, the gap is applied twice, because both factors are the same number.



The Base Method for multiplication (a+p)(a+q) adjusts by the other factor's gap. For squaring, both factors are identical: (a+p)(a+p). The cross-add step becomes: adjust by the same gap again. The right part is p² — the gap squared.



BEHOLD!

(a + p)²  =  a(a + 2p)  +  p²

LEFT: a + 2p = the number plus its own gap once more  (or: the number adjusted by its gap, as in the Base Method)

RIGHT: p² = the gap squared, in exactly as many digits as the base has zeros

This is Yāvadūnam: the deficiency (or excess) applied to itself.

❖  “Behōl! The geometry and the algebra are the same thing.”  —  Bhāskara II, 1150 CE



Pattern Two — All Bases



PATTERN 2

97² Base 100

97 squared. Gap from 100 is −3. Adjust 97 by its gap once more: 97 − 3 = 94. Square the gap: 9. Two zeros in base 100 → right part is 09.

────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────

▶  SEE  —  Look before you think

  97 near base 100:

       gap = −3       

        ↓            

  ──────●────────────●

        97          100

  Adjust: 97 − 3 = 94  |  Gap squared: 3² = 09



▶  UNDERSTAND  —  Name the pattern

Base:  100. Gap: −3. Yāvadūnam: adjust by gap once more.

LEFT:  97 + (−3) = 94  (the same as: 97 − 3 = 94)

RIGHT:  (−3)² = 9 → 2 digits → 09



▶  SOLVE  —  Let the pattern do the work

GAP

−3 from 100







LEFT

97 − 3 = 94

(adjust by gap again)





RIGHT

(−3)² = 9

2 digits → 09





ANSWER

94  |  09

join parts

9409  ✓





WHY DOES THIS WORK?  97² = (100−3)² = 100² − 2×100×3 + 9 = 10000 − 600 + 9 = 9409. Yāvadūnam compresses this to one step: adjust and square.

VERIFY A DIFFERENT WAY  97 × 97: use Base Method with both gaps = −3. Left: 97−3 = 94. Right: (−3)(−3) = 09. Answer: 9409. Identical.

❖  The gap is −3. The deficiency is 3. Yāvadūnam says: whatever the deficiency, apply it twice — once to adjust, once to square.

✓

Behold! The geometry and the algebra are the same thing. The diagram is the proof.



PATTERN 2  103²  —  gap +3, base 100

Gap:  +3 from 100.

LEFT:  103 + 3 = 106.

RIGHT:  3² = 09 (2 digits).

ANSWER:  10609  ✓

VERIFY:  Above 100, answer exceeds 10000. 10609 > 10000. ✓



PATTERN 2  998²  —  gap −2, base 1000

Gap:  −2 from 1000. Three zeros in base 1000 → right part is 3 digits.

LEFT:  998 − 2 = 996.

RIGHT:  (−2)² = 4 → 3 digits → 004.

ANSWER:  996004  ✓



PATTERN 2  1007²  —  gap +7, base 1000

Gap:  +7 from 1000.

LEFT:  1007 + 7 = 1014.

RIGHT:  7² = 49 → 3 digits → 049.

ANSWER:  1014049  ✓



PATTERN 2  9996²  —  gap −4, base 10000

Gap:  −4 from 10000. Four digits in right part.

LEFT:  9996 − 4 = 9992.

RIGHT:  (−4)² = 16 → 4 digits → 0016.

ANSWER:  99920016  ✓

NOTE:  Yāvadūnam works for any base. The rule is always: adjust by gap once more; square the gap; pad to base-zeros digits.



4.  Pattern Three — Squaring Near 50



For numbers near 50, combine Yāvadūnam with the Sub-Base 50 ratio. The gap is measured from 50; the squared gap forms the right part; the left part is scaled by ½. The procedure is identical to Chapter Six's sub-base method, with both factors equal.



PATTERN 3  47²  —  sub-base 50, gap −3

Gap from 50:  −3.

LEFT (Yāvadūnam):  47 + (−3) = 44  (adjust by gap once more)

RIGHT:  (−3)² = 09.

Scale left:  44 ÷ 2 = 22 remainder 0. No adjustment.

ANSWER:  22 | 09 = 2209  ✓

VERIFY:  47² = 2209. ✓



PATTERN 3  53²  —  sub-base 50, gap +3

Gap from 50:  +3.

LEFT:  53 + 3 = 56.  RIGHT: 3² = 09.

Scale:  56 ÷ 2 = 28 remainder 0.

ANSWER:  28 | 09 = 2809  ✓



PATTERN 3  46²  —  sub-base 50, gap −4

Gap from 50:  −4.

LEFT:  46 + (−4) = 42.  RIGHT: 4² = 16.

Scale:  42 ÷ 2 = 21 remainder 0.

ANSWER:  21 | 16 = 2116  ✓



PATTERN 3  58²  —  sub-base 50, gap +8 with remainder

Gap from 50:  +8.

LEFT:  58 + 8 = 66.  RIGHT: 8² = 64.

Scale:  66 ÷ 2 = 33 remainder 0.

ANSWER:  33 | 64 = 3364  ✓

NOTE:  58² = (60−2)² = 3600 − 240 + 4 = 3364. ✓ Alternative: near base 60, but sub-base 50 also works cleanly here.



5.  Pattern Four — The Difference of Squares



The difference of squares is not merely a squaring technique — it is the master identity underlying the mixed case of the Base Method, the symmetric sub-base cases, and many of the special products in this chapter. When two numbers are equidistant from a central value, their product is the square of the centre minus the square of the gap. The geometric proof is Bhāskara's most cited.



BEHOLD!

(a + p)(a − p)  =  a² − p²

A square of side a. Remove an L-shaped strip of total area 2ap − p².

Rearrange the L-strip into a rectangle. The rectangle has dimensions (a+p) × (a−p).

Area preserved: (a+p)(a−p) = a² − p².

The algebra is the geometry. The rearrangement is the proof.

❖  “Behōl! The geometry and the algebra are the same thing.”  —  Bhāskara II, 1150 CE



Procedure: identify the central value a and the common gap p. Compute a². Compute p². Subtract. The recognition of the pattern — that two numbers are equidistant from a centre — is the entire skill.

p

a² − p²  =  ANSWER

PATTERN 4

Diff. Squares

4997 × 5003. These two numbers are 3 below and 3 above 5000. The difference of squares: 5000² − 3². That is the entire calculation.

────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────

▶  SEE  —  Look before you think

  4997 and 5003 are equidistant from 5000:

     −3               +3           

      ↓                ↑           

  ────●────────────────●           

    4997             5000          5003

  a = 5000,  p = 3.  a² − p²  =  25000000 − 9



▶  UNDERSTAND  —  Name the pattern

Pattern:  (a+p)(a−p) = a² − p². Identify a = 5000, p = 3.

Recognition:  This requires no calculation — only the observation that the two numbers are symmetric about 5000.

Arithmetic:  5000² = 25,000,000.  3² = 9.



▶  SOLVE  —  Let the pattern do the work

IDENTIFY

a = 5000,  p = 3

equidistant





a²

5000² = 25,000,000







p²

3² = 9







ANSWER

25,000,000 − 9

=

24,999,991  ✓





WHY DOES THIS WORK?  (5000+3)(5000−3) = 5000² − 3². The identity. Tirthaji noted: "The student has been multiplying numbers. The algebra reveals they have been computing areas all along."

VERIFY A DIFFERENT WAY  4997 × 5000 − 4997 × 3 = 24985000 − 14991 = 24999... verify: 24985000 + 15000 − 9 = 25000000 − 9 = 24999991. ✓

❖  Eight-digit answer. One multiplication (trivial: 5000² = 25 million, known by inspection). One subtraction: minus 9. The recognition that a = 5000 and p = 3 is the calculation.

✓

Behold! The geometry and the algebra are the same thing. The diagram is the proof.



6.  Special Products — The Antyayoḥ Pattern



When two numbers share the same leading digits and their last digits sum to 10, the product has a fixed structure: left part = leading digits × (leading digits + 1), right part = product of last digits. This is a direct generalisation of Pattern 1 (squares ending in 5), with a + b = 10 replacing the fixed right part 25.



BEHOLD!

(10n + a)(10n + b)  =  100n(n+1)  +  ab  —  when a + b = 10

Same structure as (10n+5)² = 100n(n+1) + 25, but with ab replacing 25.

The condition a + b = 10 replaces the condition a = b = 5.

Ekādhikena Pūrvena governs both: multiply by one more than the previous.

❖  “Behōl! The geometry and the algebra are the same thing.”  —  Bhāskara II, 1150 CE



ANTYAYOḤ PATTERN  ·  Same Leading Digits, Last Digits Sum to 10

Leading × (leading+1), then last×last. Verify last digits sum to 10.

47×43  4×5=20  ·  7×3=21

2021

63×67  6×7=42  ·  3×7=21

4221

82×88  8×9=72  ·  2×8=16

7216

93×97  9×10=90  ·  3×7=21

9021

124×126  12×13=156  ·  4×6=24

15624

65×65  6×7=42  ·  5×5=25

4225



Note 65 × 65: the last digits are both 5, so 5 + 5 = 10 ✓. The right part is 5 × 5 = 25. This is Pattern 1 (squaring ending in 5) as a special case of the Antyayoḥ pattern. The two identities are the same identity. The unification is the algebra.



Look at the Antyayoḥ rapid practice table. Look at Pattern 1's table. They follow the same structure. The left part is always n × (n+1). The right part is always the product of the last-digit pair. In Pattern 1, the last-digit pair is always (5, 5). In the Antyayoḥ pattern, it is any pair summing to 10. The generalisation costs no additional procedure — only one additional observation: that the condition a + b = 10 encompasses a = b = 5 as a special case. Bhāskara would say: Behōl!

MENTOR'S SANCTUARY  ·  Parent · Teacher · Mentor · Chapter Seven

This Chapter's Purpose

Chapter Seven is where the Vedic methods begin to feel astonishing. The difference of squares identity — 97 × 103 = 9991 in two seconds — typically produces a genuine moment of mathematical wonder in the student. This is the dopamine-encoding event this book is designed to create. When it arrives, name it: "You just used algebra you did not know you knew." That sentence prepares the student for Chapter Fourteen more effectively than any algebraic instruction.

The Tradition on Teaching This

"A well-constructed diagram, properly seen, needs no further explanation." — This is Bhāskara's pedagogical claim, and it is the governing principle of this chapter. No pattern in Chapter Seven should be stated as a rule before the student has seen the diagram. The diagram is the proof. The rule is the abstraction from the diagram, not its replacement. If a student memorises the Pattern 1 rule without seeing the area model, they have learned a trick. If they have seen the model, the rule is a summary of something they have already understood.

Practical Coaching Note

For the Level 5 geometric reconstruction: do not show the student the diagram from the book. Let them attempt it from memory. An imperfect diagram they drew themselves is worth more than a perfect diagram they copied. The act of deciding where to draw the first line, what to label, which regions correspond to which terms — that act is understanding.

For the difference of squares: after the student has computed 97 × 103 = 9991 using the identity, present a new pair (say 48 × 52) and ask only: "Is this a difference of squares situation?" Do not ask for the calculation. Ask only for the recognition. The skill of spotting the pattern is more valuable than the arithmetic.

Diagnostic Flowchart — Chapter Seven

If  Student applies Ekādhikena correctly but cannot prove why  →

Work through the algebra together: write (10n+5)², expand, and show the 100n(n+1)+25 form. Ask: "What does 100n(n+1) look like written out in decimal?"

If  Student confuses Yāvadūnam with the Base Method  →

Correct: they are the same operation. Yāvadūnam is the Base Method with both factors equal. Ask: "If you use the Base Method on 97×97, what happens at the cross-add step?" The answer reveals the connection.

If  Student cannot spot the difference of squares situation  →

Practise spotting without calculating: give five pairs orally, ask only "is this a difference of squares situation?" before any arithmetic. The recognition reflex is the skill.





♦  ♦  ♦



COMING NEXT  ·  Chapter Eight — Two-Digit Crosswise Multiplication

Chapter 8 — Two-Digit Crosswise Multiplication: Ūrdhva-Tiryagbhyām

The jewel of the system. The sūtra that computes vertically and crosswise — and in doing so, computes in parallel. The pattern that modern GPU architecture independently rediscovered. Stage Four begins: two-digit multiplication in three steps, three-digit in five, extended to arbitrary length.





STAGE FOUR  ·  CROSSWISE MASTERY  ·  CHAPTER EIGHT

Chapter Eight

Two-Digit Crosswise

Ūrdhva-Tiryagbhyām  ·  Vertically and Crosswise  ·  Parallel Computing

"The crosswise method does not compute sequentially.



Diagram 4  ·  Two-digit crosswise grid — vertical arrows give end products; diagonal crossing arrows give the middle product.

It sees the whole multiplication at once — and sees it in parallel."  —  GaṇitSūtram · Stage Four





TENS  (A, C)

UNITS  (B, D)

Product

Top

A

B

L=A×C

Bot

C

D

R=B×D

Middle

Cross: A×D + B×C  =  M

M

Answer  =  L | M | R   (carry if any digit > 9)





❧

Modern processors need to perform millions of multiplications simultaneously — one for each pixel in a rendered frame, each weight in a neural network, each element of a matrix transform. A sequential algorithm handles them one at a time. This is too slow. The engineering solution is to build a chip that computes all partial products simultaneously, one circuit per product, a summation layer collecting the results by position.

The architecture this requires — compute all partial products simultaneously, sum by position, carry as needed — is precisely the structure of Ūrdhva-Tiryagbhyām. Tirthaji did not anticipate the silicon chip. He described the mathematical structure that any parallel multiplier must have. Since the 1990s, Vedic multiplier architectures based on this structure have been implemented in VLSI design and documented in IEEE and IOSR peer-reviewed research. The mathematical idea is ancient. The hardware to implement it belongs to our time.

This chapter teaches the crosswise method for two-digit multiplication. Three partial products. Three positions. All computable simultaneously. The student who reaches fluency here can multiply any two-digit pair mentally. That is a real accomplishment: it means no two-digit multiplication is beyond their reach.



CHAPTER AT A GLANCE

Chapter Eight introduces Ūrdhva-Tiryagbhyām — the most general multiplication method in this book. Unlike the Base and Sub-Base methods, which require numbers near a convenient reference, the crosswise method works for any two numbers of any size.

◆  Section 1  —  Observation Lab 6: discovering the three partial products independently

◆  Section 2  —  The sūtra Ūrdhva-Tiryagbhyām: etymology, arrows, structure

◆  Section 3  —  The three partial products: RIGHT, MIDDLE, LEFT — the locked framework

◆  Section 4  —  Worked examples: 23×14, 87×56, 99×99 with full carry tracking

◆  Section 5  —  The modern relevance: parallel computing and GPU architecture

◆  Section 6  —  The algebraic proof: (10A+B)(10C+D) as a positional expansion

2.  The Sūtra Ūrdhva-Tiryagbhyām



SUTRA  ·  3

ऊर्ध्वतिर्यग्भ्याम्

Ūrdhva-Tiryagbhyām

──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────

ETYMOLOGY  ·  Root by Root

Ūrdhva  — vertically, upward — the direction of the units×units and tens×tens products.

Tiryak  — crosswise, diagonally — the direction of the mixed-digit products.

Bhyām  — dual instrumental suffix — by means of both: vertically AND crosswise. The method requires both movements simultaneously.



ENGLISH MEANING

"Vertically and Crosswise"

PRIMARY DOMAIN

Universal multiplication of any two numbers · Matrix products · Polynomial multiplication · Parallel computation

IN THIS CHAPTER

In this chapter: Ūrdhva-Tiryagbhyām gives the three partial products of any two-digit multiplication. Vertical products occupy the units and hundreds positions; the crosswise sum occupies the tens position. The three positions are independent and can be computed simultaneously.



The sūtra names the two directions of the multiplication arrows, but its deeper meaning is architectural: all positions are computed at once. The method does not proceed from right to left, accumulating partial sums. It identifies what belongs in each position and places it there directly. This is the parallel structure.



3.  The Three Partial Products



For two two-digit numbers AB and CD (where A, B, C, D are individual digits of the tens and units positions):



RIGHT

units position

MIDDLE

tens position

LEFT

hundreds position

B × D

vertical product
of units digits

A×D + B×C

crosswise products
of mixed digits

A × C

vertical product
of tens digits



THE CROSSWISE IDENTITY

(10A+B)(10C+D)  =  100·AC  +  10·(AD+BC)  +  BD

The hundreds position receives AC (left vertical). The tens position receives AD+BC (crosswise). The units position receives BD (right vertical). This is standard algebraic expansion of a two-digit product, organised by positional value.



CROSSWISE IDENTITY — (10A+B)(10C+D) = 100AC + 10(AD+BC) + BD

100·AC

Hundreds × Hundreds

10·AD

Hundreds × Units

10·BC

Tens × Hundreds

BD

Units × Units

= 100AC + 10(AD+BC) + BD

BEHŌLD!  Every multiplication is a rectangle. Every rectangle is four areas. This IS Ūrdhva-Tiryagbhyām.

5.  The Modern Relevance — Parallel Computing



The standard column multiplication algorithm processes one digit pair at a time, from right to left, accumulating partial rows. It is inherently sequential: each step depends on the carry from the previous step. Ūrdhva-Tiryagbhyām identifies all partial products simultaneously and assigns them to positions independently. The positions do not depend on each other until the final carry propagation.



URDHVA-TIRYAGBHYAM AS PARALLEL ARCHITECTURE

Sequential (column algorithm): five operations for AB×CD: B×D, B×C (shifted), A×D (shifted), A×C (shifted), then sum all rows. Each step depends on the previous.

Parallel (Ūrdhva): three independent computations: BD, AD+BC, AC. Each computable simultaneously. One carry-propagation pass to join them.

❖  A modern parallel processor implements this structure. For a large matrix multiplication, a parallel chip with thousands of cores — each computing one partial product simultaneously — completes the operation orders of magnitude faster than a sequential processor. The Ūrdhva principle — see all products at once — describes exactly what parallel hardware must do. The mathematical idea is ancient. The circuit that runs it is modern.



Tirthaji’s Ūrdhva-Tiryagbhyām was not “relevant to” parallel computing. It describes the same mathematical structure that every parallel multiplier must implement — stated as a compressed Sanskrit principle, fifteen centuries before the circuit that would run it existed. The mathematical structure is identical. The sūtra does not say “compute from right to left.” It says: vertically and crosswise. Both at once. That is the parallel instruction.



6.  The Algebraic Proof



The crosswise method is not a trick. It is the standard algebraic expansion of a two-digit product, organised by positional value:



(10A+B)(10C+D) = 100·AC + 10·(AD+BC) + BD

The hundreds position receives AC. The tens position receives AD+BC. The units position receives BD. This is polynomial multiplication with x=10, evaluated at x=10.

For 87×56: A=8, B=7, C=5, D=6.

100×(8×5) + 10×(8×6 + 7×5) + (7×6)

= 100×40 + 10×83 + 42

= 4000 + 830 + 42 = 4872  ✓

❖  Every time a student applies the crosswise method, they are performing this algebraic expansion. Chapter Fourteen will show them that they have been doing algebra since Chapter Eight.

MENTOR'S SANCTUARY  ·  Parent · Teacher · Mentor · Chapter Eight

This Chapter's Purpose

Chapter Eight introduces the most general multiplication method in this book. Unlike the Base and Sub-Base methods, which require numbers to be near a convenient reference, the crosswise method works for any two numbers of any size. A student who reaches fluency here can multiply any two-digit pair mentally. This is a real accomplishment — name it explicitly when it is achieved.

The Tradition on Teaching This

"The method computes vertically and crosswise simultaneously." This is not a mnemonic. It is the computational architecture. A student who truly grasps that the three positions are independent — that the tens position does not wait for the units position to finish — has understood why this method is powerful. That understanding is what separates fluency from rote application.

Practical Coaching Note

The most common error is mis-placing the middle product. Students often confuse which pair is crosswise (A×D and B×C) with which is vertical (A×C and B×D). Before any calculation, require the student to name the three products by type: "What are the vertical products? What are the crosswise products?" If they can name them, they can calculate.

For the parallel computing connection at Level 4: this is a genuine intellectual connection worth exploring. A student with any interest in computer science will find this chapter’s link to GPU architecture a significant motivator. The connection is not a historical curiosity — it is the reason the crosswise method is computationally superior to column multiplication for hardware implementation.

Diagnostic Flowchart — Chapter Eight

If  Student consistently gets wrong middle product  →

They are computing A×C + B×D (both verticals) instead of A×D + B×C (crosswise). Draw the X-shape arrows explicitly. Ask: "Which digits are connected by the X? Those are the crosswise products."

If  Student handles small digits well but loses carries with large digits  →

Level 3 is precisely designed for this. Carry management in the crosswise method requires practice to become automatic. Allow multiple attempts — it is not a sign of misunderstanding.

If  Student asks about the GPU connection  →

This is worth exploring seriously. If the student has interest in computer science, the parallel computing architecture is a significant motivator. Do not dismiss it as a historical footnote.
