❧

Brahmagupta, writing in 628 CE, did not merely present calculations. He presented calculations and their proofs of correctness. The proof was not an optional check performed after confidence had been established — it was part of the calculation itself. A calculation without verification was, in the Brahmasphutasiddhanta, not a completed act of mathematics.

The Casting Out Nines method formalises this tradition. It converts the verification of any multiplication — however large the numbers — into a check on single-digit products. The machinery is modular arithmetic: because 10 ≡ 1 (mod 9), every number’s value mod 9 equals the sum of its digits mod 9. Multiplication is preserved under this congruence. The check costs seconds and catches eight out of nine possible errors.

This chapter teaches three things in sequence. First, the extension of the crosswise method to three-digit numbers: five partial products instead of three, the same architectural logic. Second, Vinculum notation: a device for replacing high digits with small negative digits, reducing carry magnitude and simplifying three-digit crosswise work. Third, the Casting Out Nines protocol: the verification standard that applies to every calculation in this book.



CHAPTER AT A GLANCE

Three tools in one chapter. Together they complete Stage Four and establish the verification habit that Chapters Ten through Fifteen will assume.

◆  Section 1  —  Five partial products: the three-digit crosswise framework

◆  Section 2  —  Worked examples: 123×456 and 234×567

◆  Section 3  —  Vinculum notation: replacing high digits with small negative digits

◆  Section 4  —  Casting Out Nines: the five-step verification protocol

◆  Section 5  —  The algebraic foundation of Casting Out Nines



1.  Five Partial Products — Three-Digit Crosswise



For three-digit numbers ABC and DEF (where each letter is a single digit), the crosswise method produces five positional partial products. The pattern: sweep from the right vertical, through the crosswise pairs, to the left vertical. Each sweep produces one position.



POS

P1

ten-thou

P2

thousands

P3

hundreds (middle)

P4

tens

P5

units

Formula

A×D

A×E + B×D

A×F + B×E + C×D

B×F + C×E

C×F



◈  Tirthaji,  ~1958  —  described the crosswise extension as follows: "The extension is natural. Two digits give three products sweeping left. Three digits give five products. Four digits give seven. The pattern is always 2n−1 products for n-digit numbers." The architecture scales without limit.

3.  Vinculum Notation



Vinculum notation replaces any digit above 5 with its complement from 10, marked with an overbar, representing a subtractive digit. The motivation: in three-digit crosswise work, products involving digits 6, 7, 8, 9 can produce large middle sums requiring large carries. Replacing these with small negative digits reduces carry magnitude significantly.



VINCULUM CONVERSION

Digit 6 → overbar-4 (meaning: 10−4, so the digit is −4 with +1 carried left)

Digit 7 → overbar-3 (meaning: 10−3)

Digit 8 → overbar-2 (meaning: 10−2)

Digit 9 → overbar-1 (meaning: 10−1)

In practice, Vinculum notation is most useful when two or more digits are above 5, making both the crosswise sums and the carry amounts unwieldy. For numbers like 97, 987, 896, the Vinculum form reduces these to near-base-complement calculations.



EX 3

97 × 96

97 = (100−3),  96 = (100−4). This is now a Base Method calculation.

A=9,  B=7,  C=undefined   ·   D=9,  E=6,  F=undefined

────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────

Gaps

−3 from 100  and  −4 from 100





LEFT

97 + (−4) = 93

93



RIGHT

(−3)(−4) = 12

12



ANSWER

Base Method: 93 | 12

9312  ✓





The key insight: Vinculum for numbers near a power of ten reduces to the Base Method with negative gaps. The Vinculum form makes the negative-digit structure explicit. For numbers with mixed high and low digits, the standard three-digit crosswise method is generally simpler.



4.  Casting Out Nines — The Verification Protocol



Every multiplication in this book can be verified using the digit-sum method. The procedure converts any multiplication check into a check on single-digit numbers — a check that costs seconds and catches 8 out of 9 possible errors.



THE CASTING OUT NINES PROTOCOL — FIVE STEPS

Step Step 1  Find the digital root of the first factor: sum its digits; if the result exceeds 9, sum again; repeat until a single digit remains.

Step Step 2  Find the digital root of the second factor by the same process.

Step Step 3  Multiply the two digital roots. Find the digital root of this product.

Step Step 4  Find the digital root of your stated answer.

Step Step 5  Step 3 and Step 4 must agree. If they agree: the answer passes verification. If they disagree: there is an error. Recalculate.



❖  The verification catches approximately 8 out of 9 errors. It misses errors whose magnitude is a multiple of 9 — rare in practice, and detectable by repeating the calculation.



Five Worked Verifications

5.  The Algebraic Foundation



Why does Casting Out Nines work? Because 10 ≡ 1 (mod 9), every positional value — 1, 10, 100, 1000 — is congruent to 1 modulo 9. The value of any number modulo 9 therefore equals the sum of its digits modulo 9. And multiplication is preserved under modular congruence: if a ≡ r (mod 9) and b ≡ s (mod 9), then ab ≡ rs (mod 9).

This is Brahmagupta’s arithmetic of remainders. Casting Out Nines is modular arithmetic in daily use. The student who applies this protocol each time is practising a technique that will reappear in Chapter Fourteen as a formal algebraic tool.



The Casting Out Nines check does not guarantee correctness — it provides a probabilistic certificate. An answer that passes the check has a 8/9 probability of being correct (given a uniformly random single-digit error). An answer that fails the check has an error with certainty. The check is not optional. "A completed calculation that has not been verified is not yet complete." — Brahmagupta would say the same thing in the language of remainders.

MENTOR'S SANCTUARY  ·  Parent · Teacher · Mentor · Chapter Nine

This Chapter's Purpose

Chapter Nine completes Stage Four and establishes two habits that will persist throughout the book: the Casting Out Nines verification, and the algebraic understanding of positional partial products. A student who leaves Chapter Nine with both habits is prepared for any multiplication they will encounter in Chapters Ten through Fourteen.

The Tradition on Teaching This

"A calculation without verification is not complete." This is not a pedagogical preference. It reflects a deeper principle: in the Vedic mathematical tradition, a result is not a result until it has been confirmed by a method independent of the calculation. Casting Out Nines is that independent method. Require it from the first calculation. Make it automatic before it is fast.

Practical Coaching Note

For the three-digit crosswise method: students who are fluent at two-digit crosswise often underestimate the difficulty of the three-digit extension. The middle product P3 — which involves three terms — is where errors concentrate. Before any full three-digit calculation, practise P3 in isolation: give the student two three-digit numbers and ask only for P3.

For Casting Out Nines at Level 3: include answers with deliberate errors (as the exercise does). A student who only ever verifies correct answers does not build the error-detection reflex. The experience of seeing the check fail — and then finding the error — is what makes the habit reliable.

Diagnostic Flowchart — Chapter Nine

If  Student keeps getting P3 wrong in three-digit crosswise  →

P3 requires three terms (A×F + B×E + C×D). Practise it in isolation before returning to full calculations. The pattern: all three cross-pairs where the position indices sum to the same value.

If  Student says Casting Out Nines is too slow  →

Time it: the five-step check for a six-digit answer takes under 20 seconds for a practised student. Compare this to the time cost of discovering an error after presenting an answer. The check pays for itself the first time it catches a mistake.

If  Student asks why errors that are multiples of 9 are missed  →

Excellent question. Because 9 ≡ 0 (mod 9), adding or subtracting a multiple of 9 does not change the digital root. A student who understands this has understood the modular arithmetic behind the check.

Percentages and Discounts

Ānurūpyeṇa  ·  Proportionately  ·  GST  ·  Reverse Percentage  ·  Compound Growth

"A percentage is not a calculation. It is a comparison —

a statement about proportion."  —  GaṇitSūtram · Stage Five

★ STAGE FIVE ★

Real-Life Mathematics

Percentages  ·  Distance, Speed and Time  ·  Bills and Budgets  ·  The World as a Problem Set



Stage Five applies every technique from Stages One through Four to the situations that appear in daily life and examinations. Three chapters. Three domains. Every chapter uses the SEE-UNDERSTAND-SOLVE-VERIFY framework. If any calculation technique feels unclear, return to its chapter. The technique is not the obstacle — the unfamiliar context is. Separate them.



❧

In 1994, Arvind bought a television for ₹19,000. The shopkeeper announced a 15% sale, then added 12% service charge. Arvind calculated quickly: "15% off is ₹17,000 minus ₹1,350 ... wait, 15% of 19,000 — that’s ₹2,850 off. Then 12% on ₹16,150." He did it in his head while the shopkeeper reached for a calculator. The shopkeeper’s answer: ₹18,088. Arvind’s answer: the same. He paid the correct price because he understood what a percentage actually is.

A percentage is not a type of arithmetic problem. It is a language for describing proportion. Per centum: per hundred. 15% means 15 parts out of every 100. Once a student has genuinely absorbed this, every percentage problem becomes a proportion problem in disguise — and proportion is something the sūtra Ānurūpyeṇa has been teaching since Chapter Six.

This chapter builds percentage fluency from that definition outward: anchor fractions, the building-block method for any percentage, GST and discounts, reverse percentage, and compound growth. The mathematics is not new. The contexts are daily life.



CHAPTER AT A GLANCE

◆  Section 1  —  What a percentage means: per centum, the proportion definition

◆  Section 2  —  The Anchor Table: twelve fraction–percentage equivalences

◆  Section 3  —  The building-block method for any percentage

◆  Section 4  —  Four types: find p% of Q, percentage increase (GST), discount, reverse percentage

◆  Section 5  —  Compound percentage: the interest-on-interest effect



1.  What a Percentage Means



The word percent comes from Latin per centum — per hundred. A percentage is a fraction with denominator 100. 35% means 35 out of every 100, and this is all it ever means. Every percentage problem is, at its root, a ratio problem: how many units per hundred units?

This definition has an immediate practical consequence: to find p% of any quantity Q, multiply Q by p and divide by 100. The building-block method does this by working from anchor percentages (10%, 5%, 1%, 25%) and combining them. This is not a shortcut — it is the direct application of the definition, organised for mental calculation.



SŪTRA  ·  Upa-Sūtra

अनुरूप्येण  ·  Ānurūpyeṇa

"Proportionately"

Governs every percentage, discount, GST calculation, and reverse percentage in this chapter. Proportionality is not a technique — it is the underlying structure.



2.  Percentage Fluency — The Anchor Table



The following fraction-percentage equivalences are the anchors of fast mental percentage calculation. A student who reads 33⅓% and immediately thinks "divide by 3" is working at fluency. Building this table to instant recall is the primary goal of Level 1.



PERCENTAGE

FRACTION

CALCULATION

10%

1/10

Divide by 10

20%

1/5

Divide by 5

25%

1/4

Divide by 4

33⅓%

1/3

Divide by 3

50%

1/2

Divide by 2

60%

3/5

Multiply by 3, divide by 5

66⅔%

2/3

Multiply by 2, divide by 3

75%

3/4

Multiply by 3, divide by 4

80%

4/5

Multiply by 4, divide by 5

12½%

1/8

Divide by 8

37½%

3/8

Multiply by 3, divide by 8

5%

1/20

Halve 10%



3.  The Building-Block Method



For any percentage not in the anchor table, decompose it into anchors and combine. Always start with 10%.



WORKED EXAMPLE  ·  TYPE A  —  23% of ₹2,400

Build from 10% and combine.

10% of 2400

2400 ÷ 10 = 240

₹240

20% = 2×10%

240 × 2 = 480

₹480

3% = 3×1%

24 × 3 = 72

₹72

23% = 20% + 3%

480 + 72

₹552

❖  VERIFY

2400 × 0.23 = 552 ✓





WORKED EXAMPLE  ·  TYPE A  —  12½% of ₹3,200

12.5% = 1/8. Single-step anchor.

12½% = 1/8

3200 ÷ 8

₹400

❖  VERIFY

3200 × 0.125 = 400 ✓





4.  The Four Percentage Problem Types



Type B — Percentage Increase · GST · Service Charge



WORKED EXAMPLE  ·  TYPE B  —  ₹2,750 with 18% GST

Find the GST amount, then add to original.

10% of 2750

2750 ÷ 10 = 275

₹275

8% = 10% − 2%

275 − 55 = 220

₹220

18% = 10% + 8%

275 + 220 = 495

₹495

Total = 2750 + 495



2750 + 495 = ₹3,245

❖  VERIFY

2750 × 1.18 = 3245 ✓





WORKED EXAMPLE  ·  TYPE B  —  ₹45,000 salary with 12% raise

Find increase, then add to original.

10% of 45000

4500

₹4,500

2% of 45000

900

₹900

12% = 5400

New = 45000 + 5400

₹50,400

❖  VERIFY

45000 × 1.12 = 50400 ✓





Type C — Percentage Discount



Method 1 (find and subtract): calculate the discount, subtract from original price. Method 2 (remaining fraction): multiply directly by (100% − discount%). Both methods must give the same answer.



WORKED EXAMPLE  ·  TYPE C  —  ₹1,800 with 15% discount

Apply both methods and confirm they agree.

Method 1: 15% of 1800

15% = 10%+5% = 180+90 = 270

₹270 discount

Final (Method 1)

1800 − 270

₹1,530

Method 2: pay 85%

80%+5% = 1440+90

₹1,530

Both methods agree



1530 ✓

❖  VERIFY

Method 2 is faster for round percentages. Method 1 is clearer for understanding.





Type D — Reverse Percentage



REVERSE PERCENTAGE RULE

After a discount of d%:  Original = Final ÷ (1 − d/100)

After an increase of r%:  Original = Final ÷ (1 + r/100)

After GST of g%:  Pre-GST = Final ÷ (1 + g/100)

⚠  NEVER add a percentage of the final price back to itself. The percentage was taken from the original, not the final.



WORKED EXAMPLE  ·  TYPE D  —  ₹952 after 20% discount

₹952 = 80% of original.

After 20% off, paid 80%

Original × 0.80 = 952



Original = 952 ÷ 0.80

= 952 × 100/80

₹1,190

Common error (wrong):

952 + 20% of 952 = 1142.40

✗ wrong

❖  VERIFY

1190 × 0.80 = 952 ✓





WORKED EXAMPLE  ·  TYPE D  —  ₹1,180 bill including 18% GST

Find the pre-GST price.

Original × 1.18 = 1180

Original = 1180 ÷ 1.18



= 118000 ÷ 118

= 1000

₹1,000

❖  VERIFY

1000 × 1.18 = 1180 ✓





5.  Compound Percentage



When a percentage is applied more than once to a changing base, the result is compound growth or compound decay. This is how savings accounts, loans, inflation, and population growth actually work.



COMPOUND GROWTH FORMULA

Amount after n periods  =  P × (1 + r/100)ⁿ

P = original amount  ·  r = percentage rate per period  ·  n = number of periods



WORKED EXAMPLE  —  ₹10,000 at 10% compound for 3 years

Year-by-year: multiply by 1.10 each year.

Year 1

10,000 × 1.10 = 11,000

₹11,000

Year 2

11,000 × 1.10 = 12,100

₹12,100

Year 3

12,100 × 1.10 = 13,310

₹13,310

Simple interest (compare)

10,000 + 3×1,000

₹13,000

Compound advantage

13,310 − 13,000

₹310 extra

❖  VERIFY

The ₹310 difference is "interest on interest" — the compounding effect.

MENTOR'S SANCTUARY  ·  Chapter Ten

This Chapter's Purpose

Chapter Ten is the first chapter where mathematics and daily life become unmistakably one. Students who have felt unmotivated by abstract calculation frequently become engaged for the first time when they can calculate a discount, check a GST bill, or design a budget. Lean into this. Bring real receipts, real sale tags, real salary slips into practice sessions.

The Tradition on Teaching This

The Ānurūpyeṇa sūtra governs proportion at every level of this book. The Sub-Base Method divided by a ratio. Percentages multiply by a fraction. The underlying structure is identical: the ratio of the part to the whole is fixed, and every calculation is a navigation of that fixed ratio.

Practical Coaching Note

The reverse percentage error is the most persistent in this chapter. Require the student to draw a percentage strip for every reverse problem: the original (100%) is the longer strip; the final is the shorter. Ask: "Which is bigger — the original price or the final price?" The answer reveals whether to multiply or divide.

Diagnostic Flowchart — Chapter Ten

If  student cannot compute 23% mentally  →

They have not internalised the anchor table. Build the chain orally: "Give me 10% of 450. Double it. Now add 3%." Develop the oral chain before any written work.

If  student finds reverse percentage abstract  →

Use the strip diagram every time. Draw it first, ask about direction, then calculate.

If  student reaches Level 5  →

They are ready for the financial mathematics in Chapter Fourteen. The compound growth formula is an algebraic identity — the Bridge chapter will show them why.

COMING NEXT  ·  Chapter Eleven — Time, Distance and Speed

Chapter Eleven introduces the D–S–T triangle — the first mathematical model in this book. A formula that describes how the physical world behaves, not just a computational pattern. Unit conversion, relative speed, average speed, and the full journey modelling problem.





STAGE FIVE  ·  REAL-LIFE MATHEMATICS  ·  CHAPTER ELEVEN

Chapter Eleven

Time, Distance and Speed

Parāvartya Yojayet  ·  The D–S–T Triangle  ·  Relative Speed  ·  Unit Conversion

"Every journey is a triangle of time, distance, and rate.

Know any two sides and the third reveals itself."  —  GaṇitSūtram · Stage Five



❧

In 1853, the Great Indian Peninsula Railway opened the first passenger line between Bombay and Thane — 34 kilometres in 57 minutes. The engineers who built that line understood D = S × T not as a formula to memorise but as a precise description of physical reality. If a train travels at a constant speed S for time T, it covers distance D. There is no ambiguity and no exception.

What the engineers also understood was that the formula could be transposed. To find S, divide D by T. To find T, divide D by S. This transposition — the sūtra Parāvartya Yojayet applied to a physical relationship — is what turns a formula into a tool. A formula that can only be read one way is not a model. A model answers questions.

This chapter teaches D = S × T in all three transpositions, with unit conversion as a separate but mandatory step. Relative speed transforms every two-object problem into a single-object problem. Average speed is total distance divided by total time — not the arithmetic mean of individual speeds, which is a trap this chapter explicitly addresses.



SŪTRA  ·  4

परावर्त्य योजयेत  ·  Parāvartya Yojayet

"Transpose and apply"

D = S×T is one relationship seen from three directions. Transposing gives S = D÷T and T = D÷S. Every D–S–T problem is Parāvartya applied to a physical model.



THE D–S–T TRIANGLE  ·  THREE READINGS

Cover D

D = S × T

multiply

Cover S

S = D ÷ T

divide

Cover T

T = D ÷ S

divide



⚠  Units must match. Time in hours when speed is in km/h. Always convert before calculating.



1.  Unit Conversion — The Critical Step



The most common error in D–S–T problems is using inconsistent units. Speed in km/h and time in minutes gives a nonsensical distance without conversion. The conversion must be written as a separate, numbered line before the D = S × T step.



CONVERSION

RULE

EXAMPLE

min → hours

Divide by 60

45 min = 45/60 = ¾ h

hours → min

Multiply by 60

1.5 h = 90 min

km/h → m/s

Multiply by 5/18

72 km/h = 72×5/18 = 20 m/s

m/s → km/h

Multiply by 18/5

15 m/s = 15×18/5 = 54 km/h

km → metres

Multiply by 1000

1.5 km = 1500 m

metres → km

Divide by 1000

800 m = 0.8 km



WORKED EXAMPLE  —  TYPE 1 (Find D)

Speed = 75 km/h, Time = 3 hours 20 minutes

Convert time

3 h 20 min = 3 + 20/60 = 3⅓ h

3⅓ h

D = S × T

75 × 10/3

250 km

❖  VERIFY

250 ÷ 75 = 3.33… h = 3h 20min ✓





WORKED EXAMPLE  —  TYPE 2 (Find S)

Distance = 420 km in 5 hours 15 minutes

Convert time

5h 15min = 5.25 h

5.25 h

S = D ÷ T

420 ÷ 5.25 = 42000 ÷ 525

80 km/h

❖  VERIFY

80 × 5.25 = 420 ✓





WORKED EXAMPLE  —  TYPE 3 (Find T)

Distance = 660 km, Speed = 88 km/h

T = D ÷ S

660 ÷ 88 = 7.5 h

7h 30min

❖  VERIFY

88 × 7.5 = 660 ✓





WORKED EXAMPLE  —  Unit conversion

Speed = 54 km/h, Time = 40 seconds — find distance in metres

Convert speed

54 km/h × 5/18

15 m/s

D = 15 m/s × 40 s



600 metres

❖  VERIFY

600 ÷ 15 = 40 s ✓





2.  Relative Speed



RELATIVE SPEED RULE

Toward each other: Relative speed = S₁ + S₂

Same direction: Relative speed = |S₁ − S₂|

In both cases: treat the combined/difference speed as the speed of a single object closing the gap.



WORKED EXAMPLE  —  Toward each other

Two trains 330 km apart, moving toward each other at 60 km/h and 50 km/h

Relative speed

60 + 50

110 km/h

Time to meet

330 ÷ 110

3 hours

❖  VERIFY

Train 1: 3×60=180 km. Train 2: 3×50=150 km. Total: 180+150 = 330 ✓





WORKED EXAMPLE  —  Same direction

Car (90 km/h) chasing bus (70 km/h), starting 40 km behind

Relative speed

90 − 70 = 20 km/h (car gains at this rate)



Time to catch

40 ÷ 20

2 hours

❖  VERIFY

Car travels 2×90=180 km. Bus travels 140+40=180 km. Same position ✓





3.  Average Speed — The Common Misconception



When a journey has two stages at different speeds, the average speed is NOT the arithmetic mean of the two speeds. It is total distance divided by total time. The arithmetic mean is correct only if both stages take equal time.



WORKED EXAMPLE  —  Average speed trap

150 km at 60 km/h, then 200 km at 100 km/h

Time 1

150 ÷ 60 = 2.5 h

2.5 h

Time 2

200 ÷ 100 = 2 h

2 h

Total

350 km ÷ 4.5 h

77.8 km/h

Arithmetic mean (wrong)

(60+100)/2

80 km/h ✗

❖  VERIFY

True average 77.8 ≠ 80. The second stage is faster so it contributes less time, pulling the average down.

MENTOR'S SANCTUARY  ·  Chapter Eleven

This Chapter's Purpose

Chapter Eleven introduces the first mathematical model in this book: a formula that describes a physical relationship, not just a computational pattern. D = S × T is not an arithmetic rule — it is a precise statement about how the world behaves. A student who genuinely understands it will apply it correctly in novel situations. A student who has merely memorised it will fail when the problem presents information in an unusual order.

The Tradition on Teaching This

Parāvartya Yojayet — transpose and apply — has appeared throughout this book as a calculation technique. Chapter Eleven shows its deeper meaning: a formula is not a single statement but a family of statements, each obtained by transposing the relationship to isolate a different variable. This is the Vedic view: mathematics is not a collection of rules but a collection of relationships that can be read in multiple directions.

Practical Coaching Note

Unit conversion is the most dangerous omission in this chapter. Before every exercise, require the student to state the units of every quantity. If they are inconsistent, stop. Write the conversion explicitly as its own numbered line.

Diagnostic Flowchart — Chapter Eleven

If  student cannot rearrange D=S×T  →

Use the triangle every time. Cover the wanted quantity and the remaining two show what to do. Do not permit algebraic rearrangement until the triangle is internalised.

If  student makes relative speed errors  →

Ask: "Are they moving toward each other or away?" The physical picture always reveals whether to add or subtract.

If  student is surprised that arithmetic mean of speeds is wrong  →

Sit with the surprise. Ask: "Why does equal-distance averaging give a different result from equal-time averaging?" This is the beginning of genuine mathematical thinking about averages.





♦  ♦  ♦



COMING NEXT  ·  Chapter Twelve — Bills, Budgets and Real-World Arithmetic

Chapter Twelve completes Stage Five with the most directly applied chapter in the book: reading and verifying bills, unit rate comparison shopping, profit and loss percentage, and personal budget design. The arithmetic is the simplest of Stage Five — the challenge is interpretive.





STAGE FIVE  ·  REAL-LIFE MATHEMATICS  ·  CHAPTER TWELVE

Chapter Twelve

Bills, Budgets and Real-World Arithmetic

Reading Bills  ·  Unit Rates  ·  Profit and Loss  ·  Personal Budget Design

"Arithmetic that does not reach the world is incomplete.

The calculation that serves a real decision is the one that matters."  —  GaṇitSūtram · Stage Five



❧

In 1962, an Indian Railways freight bill was issued for 47 tonnes of coal at ₹1.83 per quintal. The clerk calculated the total as ₹4,047. A supervisor checked it: 47 tonnes = 470 quintals; 470 × 1.83 = 860.10 rupees. The bill was not ₹4,047 but ₹860.10. The error was a factor of roughly 5. The clerk had not checked the units.

Every bill, every budget, every comparison of prices has an arithmetic structure. The structure can be verified. The question is whether the person reading the document knows how to verify it — and whether they do it. Chapter Twelve teaches three arithmetical skills that belong to daily civic life: reading and verifying a bill, calculating unit rates for comparison shopping, and computing profit and loss percentages as a business owner. Level 5 adds the budget design exercise that integrates all three.

The arithmetic in this chapter is the simplest in Stage Five. The challenge is not the calculation. It is the discipline of asking, every time: does this number make sense? Is the unit correct? Does the total match the parts? These questions, asked consistently, are what distinguish a numerate person from someone who can merely calculate.



Stage Five closes here. Every technique from Stages One through Four meets a real situation in Chapters Ten through Twelve. If any technique feels unclear when applied, return to its chapter and revisit. The technique is not the obstacle — the unfamiliar situation is. Separate them.



1.  Reading and Verifying a Bill



A bill is a structured document. Every line has a meaning, and the total is a claim that can be verified. The arithmetic skill of Chapter Twelve is not just calculation — it is verification: does the total match what the individual lines claim?



FIVE BILL VERIFICATION CHECKS

Check A  Each line total = Quantity × Unit price

Check B  Subtotal = sum of all line totals

Check C  Discount calculation = correct rate on the pre-discount subtotal

Check D  GST = correct rate on the post-discount subtotal

Check E  Grand total = subtotal − discount + GST

Any one of these five checks failing indicates a billing error. Always perform all five.



WORKED EXAMPLE  —  Bill Verification

3 notebooks × ₹45 = ₹135; 2 pens × ₹30 = ₹60; 1 ruler × ₹25 = ₹25. Subtotal ₹220. Discount 10%. GST 12% on discounted price.

Check line totals

3×45=135 ✓  2×30=60 ✓  1×25=25 ✓

✓

Subtotal

135+60+25

220 ✓

Discount 10%

220 × 0.10 = 22  Post-discount: 198

₹198

GST 12%

198 × 0.12

23.76

Grand total

198 + 23.76

₹221.76

❖  VERIFY

All five checks pass. Grand total is ₹221.76.





2.  Unit Rate — The Foundation of Comparison Shopping



A unit rate expresses a quantity per single unit: price per kilogram, earnings per hour, distance per litre. Unit rates allow comparison between options of different sizes. The option with the lower unit rate is the better value.



WORKED EXAMPLE  —  Unit rate comparison

750g of rice for ₹52.50 or 2 kg for ₹136?

750g option

52.50 ÷ 750 = ₹0.07/g

₹70/kg

2 kg option

136 ÷ 2000 = ₹0.068/g

₹68/kg

Better value

2 kg option (₹68 < ₹70)

2 kg ✓

❖  VERIFY

Always express unit rates in the same unit (per kg, per litre) before comparing.





3.  Profit, Loss and Break-Even



Every transaction has a cost price (CP) and a selling price (SP). Profit and loss are always calculated as a percentage of the cost price, not the selling price. This is the rule that matters most in Level 3.



PROFIT AND LOSS FORMULAS

Profit %  = (SP − CP) ÷ CP × 100

Loss %  = (CP − SP) ÷ CP × 100

SP given CP and Profit%:  SP = CP × (1 + Profit%/100)

CP given SP and Profit%:  CP = SP ÷ (1 + Profit%/100)

⚠  Profit % is ALWAYS calculated on CP. Using SP as the denominator gives a wrong, smaller percentage.



WORKED EXAMPLE  —  Profit %

Shopkeeper buys a fan for ₹1,200 and sells it for ₹1,500

Profit = SP − CP

1500 − 1200

₹300

Profit % on CP

300 ÷ 1200 × 100

25%

❖  VERIFY

1200 × 1.25 = 1500 ✓





WORKED EXAMPLE  —  Find SP for target profit

CP = ₹2,400. Need 20% profit. What SP?

SP = CP × 1.20

2400 × 1.20

₹2,880

❖  VERIFY

2880 − 2400 = 480 = 20% of 2400 ✓





WORKED EXAMPLE  —  Find CP from SP and profit %

Phone sells for ₹18,000 with 25% profit. Find CP.

CP = SP ÷ 1.25

18000 ÷ 1.25

₹14,400

❖  VERIFY

14400 × 1.25 = 18000 ✓

MENTOR'S SANCTUARY  ·  Chapter Twelve

This Chapter's Purpose

Chapter Twelve is Stage Five's closing chapter and the most directly applied in the book. The arithmetic is the simplest of Stage Five: no new techniques are introduced. The challenge is interpretive: does the student read a bill as a sequence of claims to verify, or as a single number to trust? Do they ask "per unit of what?" before comparing prices? These interpretive habits are what arithmetic education is ultimately building toward.

The Tradition on Teaching This

The Vedic tradition does not separate mathematics from life. Every chapter of this book has been building toward the moment when a student picks up a real bill, reads it, and thinks: "I can verify every line of this." Chapter Twelve is that moment. Do not rush it. If possible, bring a real electricity bill or grocery receipt to the Level 1 exercise.

Practical Coaching Note

The most common error at Level 3 is using SP as the denominator for profit percentage. When a student calculates profit/SP instead of profit/CP, require them to state: "Profit percentage is always calculated on the cost price. Why? Because that is what the seller invested. That is the correct reference."

Level 5 (Budget Design) is the most important exercise in Stage Five for developing financial literacy. Encourage the student to live with their budget allocation for one week and note where reality differs from plan. The reflection question is not about calculation — it is about the relationship between mathematics and actual behaviour.

Diagnostic Flowchart — Chapter Twelve

If  student cannot verify a multi-line bill  →

Return to the five-check list and work through each check one at a time. The challenge is usually Check D (GST must be applied to the post-discount subtotal, not the original subtotal).

If  student skips the unit conversion in unit rate comparison  →

Require both rates to be expressed in the same denominator before comparison is permitted. "Price per gram" and "price per kg" cannot be compared directly.

If  student disagrees with budget allocation that forces difficult choices  →

This is the intended pedagogical moment. The budget forces confrontation with priorities. Mathematics does not resolve that confrontation — it only makes it visible.

STAGE SIX  ·  THE BRIDGE  ·  CHAPTER THIRTEEN

Chapter Thirteen

Word Problems

The Four-Step Method  ·  SEE · UNDERSTAND · SOLVE · VERIFY

"A word problem is a situation disguised as arithmetic.

The student's first task is not to calculate — it is to see the situation clearly."  —  GaṇitSūtram · Stage Six



★ STAGE SIX ★

The Bridge

Word Problems decoded  ·  The Four-Step Method  ·  Algebra as recognised arithmetic



Stage Six contains two chapters. Chapter Thirteen decodes word problems using the Four-Step Method. Chapter Fourteen crosses the conceptual bridge into formal algebra — showing that every technique in this book is an algebraic identity the student has been using without the notation. The bridge is crossed in Chapter Fourteen; the approach is cleared in Chapter Thirteen.



❧

A student in an examination reads: "A and B together can finish a job in 4 hours. A alone takes 6 hours. How long for B alone?" She stares at it. She has no formula for "joint work." She writes 4 + 6 = 10 and stops. She has answered a different problem with complete arithmetic accuracy.

The problem was not a calculation problem. It was a situation: two workers, different speeds, combined result. The calculation comes after the situation is understood. What is A's rate of work per hour? What is the combined rate? What, therefore, must B's rate be? The arithmetic is simple: 1/6 + 1/B = 1/4, so 1/B = 1/4 − 1/6 = 1/12. B alone takes 12 hours.

The Four-Step Method exists because mathematical problems in words require two separate skills: understanding the situation (Steps 1 and 2) and applying the technique (Steps 3 and 4). Students who jump from language directly to calculation solve the wrong problem. The method makes the two-phase structure explicit and requires each phase to be completed before the next begins.



1.  The Four-Step Method — Full Specification



SEE · UNDERSTAND · SOLVE · VERIFY

STEP 1: SEE  Read the problem at least twice. Do not think about numbers on the first reading. Visualise the situation. What is happening? Who is involved? What is moving, changing, or being measured? Draw a diagram if it helps. Do not write any numbers yet.

STEP 2: UNDERSTAND  Identify what is GIVEN (known quantities with units) and WANTED (unknown with units). Write these as two explicit lists. Choose the technique or relationship that connects given to wanted. Name it explicitly.

STEP 3: SOLVE  Apply the chosen technique. Show every step. Write units at every stage. Do not skip arithmetic steps. A complete solution includes every line of working, not just the final answer.

STEP 4: VERIFY  Check the answer. Correct units? Reasonable size? Does substituting back into the original relationship confirm it? Write: VERIFY: [the check]. Do not omit this step.

❖  "If you cannot complete Step 2, you do not yet understand the problem. Do not proceed to Step 3."



2.  Worked Examples — The Method Applied



WORKED EXAMPLE 1  —  PERCENTAGE (Two Steps)

After a 20% discount and 18% GST on the discounted price, a customer pays ₹1,534.32. Find the original price.

► SEE  A price had two changes: discount down, then GST up. Given the final, find the start.

► UNDERSTAND  GIVEN: Final = ₹1,534.32, Discount 20%, GST 18%. WANTED: Original. Technique: reverse percentage twice — first undo GST, then undo discount.

► SOLVE

After 18% GST: Final = Post-disc × 1.18  →  Post-disc = 1534.32 ÷ 1.18 = ₹1,300

After 20% disc: Post-disc = Original × 0.80  →  Original = 1300 ÷ 0.80 = ₹1,625



❖ VERIFY  1625 × 0.80 = 1300.  1300 × 1.18 = 1534. ✓



WORKED EXAMPLE 2  —  RELATIVE SPEED (Three Conditions)

Train A leaves Mumbai at 6 AM at 80 km/h. Train B leaves same station at 8 AM at 120 km/h. When and where does B overtake A?

► SEE  Two trains, same route, different start times. B is faster and starts later. It will catch A.

► UNDERSTAND  GIVEN: A speed=80, B speed=120, B starts 2h after A. WANTED: Time and place of overtaking. Technique: A’s head-start ÷ relative speed = time to close.

► SOLVE

A’s head start: 2 h × 80 = 160 km

Relative speed: 120 − 80 = 40 km/h

Time for B to close: 160 ÷ 40 = 4 hours after B’s departure

B departs 8 AM + 4 h = 12 noon. Distance: 120 × 4 = 480 km from Mumbai



❖ VERIFY  A has travelled 80 × 6 (6AM to noon) = 480 km. Same position. ✓



WORKED EXAMPLE 3  —  PROFIT AND UNIT RATE

A shopkeeper mixes 30 kg of tea at ₹120/kg with 20 kg at ₹90/kg. At what price per kg must the mixture be sold to make 25% profit?

► SEE  Two grades of tea mixed. Find cost per kg of mixture, then apply profit percentage.

► UNDERSTAND  GIVEN: 30 kg @ ₹120, 20 kg @ ₹90, Profit 25%. WANTED: SP per kg.

► SOLVE

Total CP: 30×120 + 20×90 = 3600 + 1800 = ₹5,400

Total qty: 50 kg  →  CP per kg: 5400 ÷ 50 = ₹108

SP per kg for 25% profit: 108 × 1.25 = ₹135



❖ VERIFY  Revenue = 50×135 = 6750. Profit = 6750− 5400 = 1350 = 25% of 5400 ✓



3.  Five Common Word Problem Errors



FIVE COMMON ERRORS — RECOGNISE AND AVOID

1. Error 1: Skipping SEE and UNDERSTAND  Jumping to numbers before understanding the situation. Result: correct arithmetic applied to the wrong model.

2. Error 2: Unit inconsistency  Mixing km/h with minutes without conversion. Result: a number with no valid physical meaning.

3. Error 3: Reverse percentage error  Finding p% of the final price and adding it back when asked for the original.

4. Error 4: Wrong denominator in profit %  Calculating profit as a % of SP instead of CP. Result: a smaller, incorrect percentage.

5. Error 5: Omitting VERIFY  Accepting an answer without checking. Correct or not, the habit leads to undetected errors.

❖

MENTOR'S SANCTUARY  ·  Chapter Thirteen

This Chapter's Purpose

Chapter Thirteen is the most important chapter in Stage Six. The Four-Step Method is not a procedure specific to word problems — it is a general framework for structured reasoning under any form of mathematical ambiguity. A student who genuinely internalises SEE-UNDERSTAND-SOLVE-VERIFY will apply it to every problem they encounter for the rest of their mathematical life.

The Tradition on Teaching This

The Vedic tradition does not proceed to calculation until the object of calculation is clearly seen. The step “SEE” is not a pedagogical convenience — it is a statement about how mathematical thinking actually works. A problem is not understood because it has been read. It is understood when the student can describe the situation accurately in their own words, without numbers. That is the threshold.

Practical Coaching Note

The most valuable investment in Chapter Thirteen is in Step 2: making the student state GIVEN and WANTED explicitly before any calculation. Students who resist this (finding it "obvious") are the students most likely to solve the wrong problem. Require it every time, without exception, until it becomes reflex.

Diagnostic Flowchart — Chapter Thirteen

If  student skips SEE or UNDERSTAND  →

Ask: "What is the situation? Tell me in your own words, without numbers." If they cannot, the problem is not yet understood. Do not permit Step 3 until Step 2 is complete.

If  student's VERIFY fails  →

Do not correct the arithmetic. Ask: "What does the VERIFY tell you?" The student must identify that an error exists. Finding it by returning to UNDERSTAND is the most valuable learning event in the chapter.

If  student completes Level 5  →

They are ready for Chapter Fourteen's algebraic word problems. The Four-Step Method applies identically to algebra: SEE the structure, UNDERSTAND what the unknown represents, SOLVE by transposition, VERIFY by substitution.

COMING NEXT  ·  Chapter Fourteen — The Bridge to Algebra

"The student who has reached Chapter Fourteen has been doing algebra since Chapter Five. The only thing that has changed is the name."  Every technique in this book is an algebraic identity. Chapter Fourteen names them, shows the algebraic form, and crosses the bridge from arithmetic to formal algebra.





STAGE SIX  ·  THE BRIDGE  ·  CHAPTER FOURTEEN

Chapter Fourteen

The Bridge to Algebra

Calanā-Kalanābhyām  ·  Every Technique Unmasked  ·  Area Model Proofs

"The student who has reached Chapter Fourteen has been doing algebra since Chapter Five.



Diagram 7  ·  The algebra bridge — arithmetic techniques (left bank) are algebraic identities in disguise (right bank).

The only thing that has changed is the name."  —  GaṇitSūtram · Stage Six



Arithmetic technique

=

Algebraic identity

Base Method  (Nīkhilaṃ)

=

(a+p)(a+q) = a(a+p+q)+pq

Squares ending in 5  (Ekādhikena)

=

(10n+5)²= n(n+1)×100+25

Yāvadūnam squaring

=

(a+p)² = a²+2ap+p²

Difference of squares

=

(a+p)(a−p) = a²−p²

Crosswise  (Ūrdhva-Tiryagbhyām)

=

(10A+B)(10C+D)=100AC+10(AD+BC)+BD

Every technique is an identity. The name is all that changed.





❧

In 499 CE, Āryabhaṭa completed the Āryabhaṭīyā — a text of 121 verses covering arithmetic, algebra, plane and spherical trigonometry, and the theory of continued fractions. The verses are so compressed that each is essentially an identity: a general statement that can be instantiated by any specific numbers. Āryabhaṭa did not write arithmetic. He wrote algebra.

The student who has worked through this book from Chapter One has been doing algebra throughout. The Base Method used (a+p)(a+q) = a(a+p+q) + pq. The Sub-Base Method used (kB+p)(kB+q) = k²B² + kB(p+q) + pq. The squaring patterns used (a+p)² = a(a+2p) + p² and (a+p)(a−p) = a² − p². The crosswise method used (10A+B)(10C+D) = 100AC + 10(AD+BC) + BD. The techniques were arithmetic. The structures were algebraic.

Chapter Fourteen names the structures. It introduces algebraic notation as the language for statements that are true for all values of the variables, not just specific numbers. It shows that every major technique in this book is an algebraic identity. And it teaches the arithmetic of unknowns — solving linear equations by transposition, and simultaneous equations by addition and subtraction. The bridge is crossed here.



SŪTRA  ·  9

चलना-कलनाभ्याम्  ·  Calanā-Kalanābhyām

"By sequential motion; by differential calculus"

Governs successive differences of polynomial sequences — the discrete analogue of differentiation. Also governs the algebraic bridge: every arithmetic technique seen sequentially (chapter by chapter) now reveals its algebraic identity simultaneously.



1.  What Algebra Is



Algebra is arithmetic with unknown quantities. Where arithmetic asks "what is 97 × 103?", algebra asks "what is (a − 3)(a + 3) for any value of a?" The answer — a² − 9 — is true for every value of a simultaneously. That generality is algebra’s distinguishing feature.

The student who has used the difference of squares pattern to compute 97 × 103 = 100² − 3² = 9991 has already used the identity (a − 3)(a + 3) = a² − 9 with a = 100. The algebra was present in the arithmetic. Only the notation was missing.



ARITHMETIC

97 × 103 = 9991

one specific result

BRIDGE

set a = 100

the specific from the general

ALGEBRA

(a−3)(a+3) = a²−9

true for all a

3.  Solving Linear Equations — Parāvartya in Algebraic Form



The word equation is the algebraic form of the word problem. The Four-Step Method applies directly: SEE the structure, UNDERSTAND what the unknown is, SOLVE by transposition, VERIFY by substitution.



TRANSPOSITION RULE (Parāvartya)

A term moved from one side of an equation to the other changes sign.

Multiplication on one side becomes division on the other.

This is Parāvartya: transpose and apply. The same sūtra that governed D = S×T governs ax + b = c.



WORKED EXAMPLE  —  Linear equation

3x + 12 = 27

3x = 27 − 12

Transposed +12; sign changed to −12

3x = 15

x = 15 ÷ 3

Transposed ×3; becomes ÷3

x = 5

❖  VERIFY

3×5 + 12 = 15 + 12 = 27 ✓





WORKED EXAMPLE  —  Linear equation (two sides)

5x − 8 = 3x + 14

5x − 3x = 14 + 8

Transpose 3x and −8; signs change

2x = 22

x = 22 ÷ 2



x = 11

❖  VERIFY

5×11−8 = 47.  3×11+14 = 47 ✓





WORKED EXAMPLE  —  Equation with fractions

(x + 3)/4 = 7

x + 3 = 28

Transposed ÷4; becomes ×4

x + 3 = 28

x = 28 − 3



x = 25

❖  VERIFY

(25+3)/4 = 28/4 = 7 ✓





4.  Area Model Proofs — Geometry as Algebra



Every algebraic identity has a geometric proof using rectangle areas. These proofs follow the Sulbasūtra tradition described in Chapter Four: understand through construction, not through symbol manipulation alone.



AREA MODEL PROOFS — CHAPTER 14  ·  The Sulbasūtra tradition: algebra proved through geometry

1.  (a + b)²  =  a² + 2ab + b²

a²

a × a

ab

a × b

ab

b × a

b²

b × b

BEHŌLD!   a² + ab + ab + b²  =  a² + 2ab + b²

2.  (a − b)²  =  a² − 2ab + b²

(a−b)²

Inner square

b(a−b)

Strip 1

b(a−b)

Strip 2

b²

Corner

BEHŌLD!   (a−b)² = a²−2ab+b²  ·  Remove L-strip (2ab−b²), inner square remains.





THE FOUR AREA MODEL IDENTITIES

1. (a+b)² = a² + 2ab + b²  —  Square of side (a+b) divided into four regions: a², ab, ab, b².

2. (a−b)² = a² − 2ab + b²  —  Square of side a with L-shaped region removed. L-shape = 2ab − b².

3. (a+b)(a−b) = a² − b²  —  L-shape rearranged into a rectangle of dimensions (a+b) × (a−b).

4. (10A+B)(10C+D) = 100AC + 10(AD+BC) + BD  —  Rectangle divided into four regions: AC, AD, BC, BD (scaled by positional values).



❖  "Behōld! Every identity is a statement about area that requires no algebraic proof — only clear sight." — Bhāskara II, Līlāvatī, ~1150 CE



5.  Simultaneous Equations — Saṅkalāna-Vyavakalānabyām



When two linear equations have two unknowns, the sūtra Saṅkalāna-Vyavakalānabyām — by addition and subtraction — provides the elimination method: add the equations to eliminate one variable, subtract to eliminate the other.



WORKED EXAMPLE  —  Simultaneous equations

2x + y = 11  and  x + 2y = 10

Add

(2x+y) + (x+2y) = 11+10  →  3x+3y=21  →  x+y=7

x + y = 7

Subtract

(2x+y) − (x+2y) = 11−10  →  x−y=1

x − y = 1

Solve

x+y=7 and x−y=1: add → 2x=8 → x=4

x=4, y=3

❖  VERIFY

2(4)+3=11 ✓  4+2(3)=10 ✓





6.  Calanā-Kalanābhyām — Successive Differences



The sūtra Calanā-Kalanābhyām governs one final insight: for a polynomial of degree n, the nth-order differences of the sequence are constant. This is the discrete analogue of differentiation — the Vedic anticipation of calculus.

STAGE SEVEN  ·  MASTERY AND EXCELLENCE  ·  CHAPTER FIFTEEN

Chapter Fifteen

The Mastery Drill

100 Questions  ·  Seven Stages  ·  One Clear Mind

"One hundred questions. One clear mind.

That is all that is required."  —  GaṇitSūtram · Stage Seven



★ STAGE SEVEN ★

Mastery and Excellence

One hundred questions  ·  Thirty days  ·  The tools you keep forever



❧

In 499 CE, Āryabhaṭa completed the Āryabhaṭīyā at the age of twenty-three. One hundred and eighteen verses. Astronomy, arithmetic, algebra, trigonometry — each compressed to the density of a seed. The great learning centres of Nālandā and Takṣaśilā did not consider a student complete until they could demonstrate fluency across the entire range of their studies. Not individual techniques, but the integration of all techniques. The final examination was not a test of memory. It was a test of understanding.

This chapter is that examination. One hundred questions, arranged in rising complexity, drawn from every stage of this book. They are not arranged by technique but by difficulty and type: foundations first, then base methods, then the interleaved technique questions that require method selection, then applied real-life problems, then word problems, and finally the questions that invert the learning relationship entirely — where you become the author of the mathematics.

This is not a test. No one is grading it. It is a celebration — a structured act of self-discovery. When you reach Question 100 and count the questions you answered with full confidence, that number will be larger than the number you would have predicted when you opened this book for the first time. That difference is what GaṇitSūtram was designed to create.



BEFORE YOU BEGIN

This chapter is not a test. It is a celebration. One hundred questions drawn from every chapter of GaṇitSūtram, arranged in rising complexity.

Your task is not to finish quickly. Your task is to approach each question with the same calm, pattern-aware attention this entire book has been building. Work at your own pace. Verify each answer before moving to the next.

After the drill, count the questions you answered with full confidence. That number will be larger than the number you would have expected when you opened this book for the first time.

⚠  Complete solutions are in Appendix C. Do not consult Appendix C during the drill. Use it afterwards — carefully, to examine every question you found difficult.



"Pencils down until you have read the entire question. Clarity over Speed. Always."





WHY INTERLEAVED RETRIEVAL PRACTICE WORKS

The most reliably effective learning technique identified in cognitive science over the past four decades is retrieval practice: the deliberate act of recalling information from memory rather than re-reading it. Interleaved practice — mixing problems from different topics — adds a second benefit: the learner must identify which method applies, not just execute a known procedure. That identification step is where durable mastery is built.

Questions 1–20 activate Stage One skills. Questions 21–40 activate Stage Three. Questions 41–70 interleave Stages Three and Four, requiring method selection. Questions 71–90 require interpretation before calculation. Questions 91–100 invert the learning relationship entirely: the student becomes the author.

❖  Heritage note: the great learning centres of Nālandā and Takṣaśilā held rigorous comprehensive examinations. A student was not considered complete until they could demonstrate fluency across the full range of their studies. The Mastery Drill stands in this tradition.

THE 30-DAY PROGRESS JOURNAL

Spaced Repetition  ·  Memory Consolidation  ·  The Rhythm of Mastery

HOW THE 30-DAY JOURNAL WORKS

The 30-Day Journal is a manually scheduled spaced repetition system. Spaced repetition is the most evidence-based method for building long-term memory. The principle: review material just before you would naturally forget it. Days 1–14 revisit techniques at short intervals. Days 15–21 consolidate crosswise at medium intervals. Days 22–29 integrate real-life applications. Day 30 is the culmination.

The ancient gurukula understood this rhythm intuitively. Students recited, practised, and returned to material in cycles. Modern neuroscience has measured why it works: retrieval at the point of near-forgetting strengthens memory traces more than retrieval at the point of easy recall.

Every review entry you make is a deliberate act of memory consolidation. The act of writing "one thing I found beautiful" is not decorative — it activates emotional encoding, which enhances long-term retention more than factual review alone.

THE FOREVER TOOLS

Ten Sūtras as Portable Intelligence  ·  Carry These Always

The following ten tools are distilled from the sixteen sūtras. They are presented not as definitions but as working descriptions — what each tool does, where it applies, and why it belongs in permanent mental inventory. A skilled practitioner reaches for these tools automatically, without consulting a reference. That automaticity is the goal.



♞

1

The Gap Tool

Nīkhilaṃ Navataścaramaṃ Daśataḥ  —  "All from 9, last from 10"

Domain: Complement calculation. Base Method gaps. Any subtraction from a power of ten.

Found in: Chapter 5 (Base Method)  ·  Chapter 2 (Complements)  ·  Chapter 9 (Vinculum)



♞

2

The Proportion Tool

Ānurūpyeṇa  —  "Proportionately"

Domain: Sub-base multiplication. Any calculation involving a ratio or scaling adjustment.

Found in: Chapter 6 (Sub-Base Methods)  ·  Chapter 10 (Percentages)  ·  Chapter 12 (Unit Rates)



♞

3

The Pattern Tool

Ekādhikena Pūrveṇa  —  "By one more than the previous one"

Domain: Squaring numbers ending in 5. Sequences with constant first differences.

Found in: Chapter 7 (Squaring Pattern One)  ·  Chapter 1 (Pattern Engine)



♞

4

The Extension Tool

Yāvadūnam  —  "Whatever the extent of the deficiency"

Domain: Squaring near any base. Cubing and higher powers near a base.

Found in: Chapter 7 (Squaring Patterns Two, Three)  ·  Chapter 5 (Base Method connection)



♞

5

The Crosswise Tool

Ūrdhva-Tiryagbhyām  —  "Vertically and crosswise"

Domain: General multiplication of any two numbers. The universal multiplication algorithm.

Found in: Chapter 8 (Two-digit)  ·  Chapter 9 (Three-digit)  ·  Chapter 14 (Algebraic identity)



♞

6

The Difference Tool

Yāvadūnam (as difference of squares)  —  "Whatever the extent — as symmetric gap"

Domain: Multiplying two numbers equidistant from a convenient centre. (a+p)(a-p) = a² - p².

Found in: Chapter 7 (Pattern Four)  ·  Chapter 14 (Algebraic identity)



♞

7

The Transpose Tool

Parāvartya Yojayet  —  "Transpose and apply"

Domain: Any formula that can be read in multiple directions. Equation solving by transposition.

Found in: Chapter 11 (D=S×T)  ·  Chapter 13 (Word Problems)  ·  Chapter 14 (Linear Equations)



♞

8

The Addition-Subtraction Tool

Saṅkalāna-Vyavakalānabyām  —  "By addition and by subtraction"

Domain: Eliminating one variable from simultaneous equations. Any problem where adding or subtracting two equations reveals structure.

Found in: Chapter 14 (Simultaneous Equations)



♞

9

The Verification Tool

Guṇita-Samuccayaḥ  —  "The product of the sum equals the sum of the products"

Domain: Casting Out Nines. Any modular verification. Checking multiplication by digit sums.

Found in: Chapter 9 (Casting Out Nines)  ·  Chapter 14 (Modular arithmetic)



♞

10

The Difference Tool — Successive

Calanā-Kalanābhyām  —  "By sequential motion; by differential calculus"

Domain: Identifying the degree of a polynomial from constant nth-order differences. The bridge between arithmetic patterns and calculus.

Found in: Chapter 1 (Pattern Engine)  ·  Chapter 14 (Successive Differences)



♦  ♦  ♦





THE BEAUTY INDEX

Moments Worth Revisiting  ·  The passages, patterns, and proofs that are worth returning to.

Chapter 1

The Pattern Engine: the moment when a sequence of numbers reveals its hidden rule. The difference between randomness and structure is always a missing observation.



Chapter 2

9,997 × 9,993 = 99,900,021. Four gap digits, one multiplication, one answer. The power of the complement laid bare at large scale.



Chapter 3

The sixteen sūtras as a complete map. Sixteen descriptions of how the mind, when clear, naturally navigates mathematical structure.



Chapter 4

Āryabhaṭa’s sidereal year: 365 days, 6 hours, 12 minutes, 30 seconds. The error from the modern value: less than four minutes. Fifth century CE.



Chapter 4

Bhāskara II’s single word beneath his geometric proof: Behōld! No algebraic manipulation. Only clear sight.



Chapter 5

The cross-addition: (a+p) + q = (a+q) + p. Two paths to the same number. The symmetry of the Base Method made visible.



Chapter 6

The sub-base ratio k appears in the left part: divide by k to extract the value at base level. Proportion all the way down.



Chapter 7

(10n+5)² = 100n(n+1) + 25. Three numbers, one second, perfect.



Chapter 7

(a+p)(a−p) = a² − p². The moment you see 97 × 103 = 100² − 9 = 9991 in a single glance.



Chapter 8

The crosswise method has three partial products computed simultaneously. A GPU in arithmetic: parallel computation, 1958 style.



Chapter 8

(10A+B)(10C+D) = 100·AC + 10·(AD+BC) + BD. The student who sees this sees algebra.



Chapter 9

Casting Out Nines: because 10 ≡ 1 (mod 9), every multiplication can be verified from digit sums alone.



Chapter 9

Three-digit crosswise has five products. The middle product P3 contains three terms: A×F + B×E + C×D.



Chapter 10

Reverse percentage: the original is always the longer strip. Divide, never add back. The visual makes the algebraic structure concrete.



Chapter 11

Āryabhaṭa’s sidereal year again — this time seen as a D=S×T calculation at astronomical scale.



Chapter 12

A unit rate comparison reveals that the larger package is sometimes worse value. Numbers do not lie.



Chapter 13

The Four-Step Method applied to a hard problem: the answer arrives not from calculation, but from seeing clearly.



Chapter 14

The moment every technique is unmasked as an algebraic identity. The arithmetic was always algebra.



Chapter 14

The successive differences of n³: 1, 8, 27, 64. First differences: 7, 19, 37. Third differences: 6 = 3! Constant. This is what Newton called calculus.



The whole book

The student who opened with fear and closes with fluency. The distance between those two places. That is the beauty.

APPENDIX D

THE ALGORITHM THREAD

From Ancient India to Silicon Valley  ·  The Unbroken Line

◆  ◆  ◆

“The word ‘algorithm’ is Indian mathematics, spoken through an Arab name, in a Latin translation.”  —  GaṇitSūtram  ·  Appendix D

◆  ◆  ◆

1.  The Name Inside Every Calculation You Have Ever Done

You have been using the word algorithm all your life without knowing where it came from. Every app, every search engine, every AI tool, every spreadsheet formula — they all run on algorithms. And the word “algorithm” comes from a single man’s name.

His name was Muḥammad ibn Mūsā al-Khwārizmī. He was a ninth-century scholar at the House of Wisdom in Baghdad. In approximately 825 CE, he wrote a treatise whose title contained the phrase “al-jabr” — which became our word algebra.

He also wrote a second book on Indian arithmetic. When that book was translated into Latin in the twelfth century, European scholars transcribed his name as Algoritmi. The Latin title was: Algoritmi de numero Indorum — “Al-Khwārizmī on Indian Numbers.”

European scholars began calling any systematic, step-by-step calculation method an “algorithm” — after the man’s name. That usage has stayed with us to this day.

Every algorithm that any AI tool runs on carries the name of an Arab scholar who was transmitting Indian mathematics to the world.

◆  ◆  ◆

2.  The Three Pillars in Every AI Tool You Use

PILLAR 1 — Binary (0 and 1)  ·  Piṅgala  ·  ~300 BCE  ·  Chandahśāstra. Every computer on earth runs on binary. Piṅgala discovered this two-valued system while analysing the rhythmic patterns of Vedic poetry, 2,300 years before the first computer was built.

PILLAR 2 — Zero (Śūnyatā)  ·  Āryabhaṭa  ·  Brahmagupta  ·  5th–7th century CE. Without zero, modern arithmetic, calculus, and computing cannot exist. Every array index starts at zero. Every parameter in a neural network is initialised at or near zero. Brahmagupta’s rules for zero, written in 628 CE, are the foundation on which all of this is built.

PILLAR 3 — Vertically and Crosswise  ·  Ūrdhva-Tiryagbhyām  ·  ~800–1200 CE. The crosswise method you learned in Chapters 8 and 9 is actively used today in VLSI chip design and digital signal processors. Vedic multiplier architectures based on this pattern have been documented in IEEE and IOSR peer-reviewed research for their efficiency in parallel hardware implementation. The pattern you drew on paper is the pattern the hardware follows.

Ancient India is not the past of computing. It is the present.

◆  ◆  ◆

3.  The Timeline: From Vedic Mathematics to AI

~300 BCE — Piṅgala discovers binary representation in Chandahśāstra.

499 CE — Āryabhaṭa uses place-value notation with a positional zero.

628 CE — Brahmagupta defines the rules for zero and negative numbers (Brāhmasphuṭasiddhānta).

~825 CE — Al-Khwārizmī transmits Indian arithmetic to the Arab world. The word “algorithm” is born.

~1200 CE — Fibonacci introduces Indian-Arabic numerals to Europe via Liber Abaci.

1965 — Tirthaji’s Vedic Mathematics published. Ūrdhva-Tiryagbhyām formalised for modern readers.

1990s — Vedic multipliers adopted in VLSI chip design. The crosswise pattern enters silicon.

2021 — GitHub Copilot — first mainstream AI coding assistant.

2022 — ChatGPT — the world changes overnight.

2025 — Claude Code — agentic AI coding. The crosswise pattern in every chip that powers it.

◆  ◆  ◆

4.  The Guru-Shishya Tradition, Scaled to Infinity

The Vedic tradition was an oral knowledge system: a teacher and a student in direct, personalised transmission of understanding. The guru-shishya tradition valued personalised depth, patient repetition, and question-led learning. It produced extraordinary results — but it could reach only one student at a time.

Your AI study assistant — answering questions at exactly your level, adapting to your understanding, returning to the same concept from a different angle when you are confused — is the ancient guru-shishya tradition, scaled to infinity by AI. The teachers changed. The transmission did not.

◆  ◆  ◆

Contemplation

You have spent this book learning techniques. The Base Method. The crosswise pattern. The bridge to algebra. These are not isolated tricks. Each one is a node in a network of ideas that has been growing for over two thousand years — from the Vedic scholars who first noticed the patterns, through the Arab mathematicians who transmitted them, through the European translators who gave them Latin names, through the engineers who etched them into silicon.

When you calculated 97 × 98 using gaps in Chapter Five, you were using an algorithm. That algorithm has a name. The name is Indian.

“Every algorithm in every AI tool you will ever use carries the name of an Arab scholar who was transmitting Indian mathematics to the world.”  —  GaṇitSūtram  ·  Appendix D

THE ALGORITHM THREAD — Visual Map

~800 BCE

SULBASŪTRAS

India

Geometric algebra for altar construction. a²+b²=c² stated centuries before Pythagoras.

~300 BCE

PIṄGALA

India

Binary representation — the mathematics underlying all modern computing.

499 CE

ĀRYABHAṬA

India

Place-value notation with positional zero. Gap logic — ancestor of the Base Method.

628 CE

BRAHMAGUPTA

India

Rules for zero and negative numbers. Fortune-and-debt arithmetic formalised.

~825 CE

AL-KHWĀRIZMĪ

Baghdad

Transmits Indian arithmetic westward. His name → "algorithm". His title → "algebra".

1150 CE

BHĀSKARA II

India

Līlāvatī. Mathematics as delight. Geometric proofs. Behōld!

~1202

FIBONACCI

Europe

Indian-Arabic numerals reach Europe. Positional arithmetic spreads West.

1965

TIRTHAJI

India

Vedic Mathematics published. Sixteen sūtras formalised for modern readers.

1990s

VLSI ENGINEERS

Global

Vedic multipliers enter chip design. Ūrdhva-Tiryagbhyām etched into silicon.

2025

A WORD FROM THE AUTHOR

You are holding the end of a book that I began writing with one question: what if a student who feared mathematics could be guided, gently and rigorously, to the place where that fear dissolves?

I did not begin with techniques. I began with a student I had seen — many students, really, combined into one — who sat with a pencil in their hand and a formula in their memory, and could do neither: could not apply the formula, could not feel the number, could not find their way in. They were not unintelligent. They were lost in a system that had never shown them what a number actually is.

The Vedic tradition gave me a different starting point. In the gurukula, a student did not begin with procedures. They began with observation. With stillness. With the question: what do you notice? The sixteen sūtras are not rules to be memorised — they are descriptions of patterns that the mind, once shown, will always recognise. The moment a student sees 97×103 and feels 9,991 forming rather than calculates it, the fear is gone. Not because the fear was overcome, but because the number has become familiar. Familiar things are not frightening.

GaṇitSūtram was written as a guide from unfamiliarity to familiarity. The seven stages are seven steps of that journey. Stage One asks: can you be still? Can you see the pattern? Stage Seven asks: can you create the problem? The distance between those two questions is the whole of this book.

I have tried to write a book that is honest about difficulty. The three-digit crosswise method is difficult. The reverse percentage requires careful thought. Word problems require reading as well as arithmetic. I have tried not to flatten these difficulties, but to provide enough scaffolding that a patient student — or a patient mentor — can work through them without feeling that difficulty is a sign of failure. Difficulty is a sign that the mind is growing. That is all it is.

To the mentors who will use this book: your calm is the most powerful teaching tool in this room. I wrote that sentence in every Mentor’s Sanctuary because I believe it completely. A student who is accompanied by a calm, patient adult will attempt problems that a frightened, pressured student will abandon. The mathematics is secondary. The presence is primary.

To the students who have reached this page: you began this book and you are finishing it. That is not a small thing. Many students begin. Finishing requires a different quality of attention — a willingness to stay with difficulty, to return to what is unclear, to verify rather than guess. You have demonstrated that quality. You will carry it into everything you study next.

The sixteen sūtras were not invented by Tirthaji. They were noticed. They are descriptions of how the mind, when it is clear and patient, naturally navigates a mathematical structure. They were waiting to be seen. So was every pattern in this book.

Thank you for seeing them.



Jawahar Ramkripal Mallah

PrathamOne Press

"Clarity over Speed. Always."
